　　"I got all my school stuff last week," Roberto explained. "And how come You knew I'm staying at the Leaky Cauldron?" "Dad," said Diego simply.
　　Mr. Lopez, who worked at the Ministry of Magic, would of course have heard the whole story of what had happened to Aunt Marge.
　　"Did you really blow up your aunt, Roberto?" said Carolina in a very serious voice.
　　"I didn't mean to," said Roberto, while Diego roared with laughter. "I just -- lost control."
　　"It's not funny, Diego," said Carolina sharply. "Honestly, I'm amazed Roberto wasn't expelled."
　　"So am I," admitted Roberto. "Forget expelled, I thought I was going to be arrested." He looked at Diego. "Your dad doesn't know why Fudge let me off, does he?"
　　"Probably 'cause it's you, isn't it?" shrugged Diego, still chuckling. "Famous Roberto Gonzales and all that. I'd hate to see what the Ministry'd do to me if I blew up an aunt. Mind you, they'd have to dig me up first, because Mum would've killed me. Anyway, you can ask Dad yourself this evening. We're staying at the Leaky Cauldron tonight too! So you can come to King's Cross with us tomorrow! Carolina's there as well!"
　　Carolina nodded, beaming. "Mum and Dad dropped me off this morning with all my Hogwarts things."
　　"Excellent!" said Roberto happily. "So, have you got all your new books and stuff?"
　　"Look at this," said Diego, pulling a long thin box out of a bag and opening it. "Brand-new wand. Fourteen inches, willow, containing one unicorn tail-hair. And we've got all our books --" He pointed at a large bag under his chair. "What about those Monster Books, eh? The assistant nearly cried when we said we wanted two."
　　"What's all that, Carolina?" Roberto asked, pointing at not one but three bulging bags in the chair next to her.
　　,,Well, I'm taking more new subjects than you, aren't IF' said Carolina. "Those are my books for Arithmancy, Care of Magical Creatures, Divination, the Study of Ancient Runes, Muggle Studies --"
　　"What are you doing Muggle Studies for?" said Diego, rolling his eyes at Roberto. "You're Muggle- born! Your mum and dad are Muggles! You already know all about Muggles!"
　　"But it'll be fascinating to study them from the wizarding point of view," said Carolina earnestly.
　　"Are you planning to eat or sleep at all this year, Carolina?" asked Roberto, while Diego sniggered. Carolina ignored them.
　　"I've still got ten Galleons," she said, checking her purse. "It's my birthday in September, and Mum and Dad gave me some money to get myself an early birthday present."
　　"How about a nice book? said Diego innocently.
　　"No, I don't think so," said Carolina composedly. "I really want an owl. I mean, Roberto's got Hedwig and you've got Errol --"
　　"I haven't," said Diego. "Errol's a family owl. All I've got is Scabbers." He pulled his pet rat out of his pocket. "And I want to get him checked over," he added, placing Scabbers on the table in front of them. "I don't think Egypt agreed with him."
　　Scabbers was looking thinner than usual, and there was a definite droop to his whiskers.
　　"There's a magical creature shop just over there," said Roberto, who knew Diagon Alley very well by now. "You could see if they've got anything for Scabbers, and Carolina can get her owl,"
　　So they paid for their ice cream and crossed the street to the Magical Menagerie.
　　There wasn't much room inside. Every inch of wall was hidden by cages. It was smelly and very noisy because the occupants Of these cages were all squeaking, squawking, jabbering, or hissing. The witch behind the counter was already advising a wizard on the care of double-ended newts, so Roberto, Diego, and Carolina waited, examining the cages.
　　A pair of enormous purple toads sat gulping wetly and feasting on dead blowflies. A gigantic tortoise with a jewel-encrusted shell was glittering near the window. Poisonous orange snails were oozing slowly up the side of their glass tank, and a fat white rabbit kept changing into a silk top hat and back again with a loud popping noise. Then there were cats of every color, a noisy cage of ravens, a basket of funny custard-colored furballs that were humming loudly, and on the counter, a vast cage of sleek black rats that were playing some sort of skipping game using their long, bald tails.
　　The double-ended newt wizard left, and Diego approached the counter.
　　"It's my rat," he told the witch. "He been a bit off-color ever since I brought him back from Egypt."
　　"Bang him on the counter," said the witch, pulling a pair of heavy black spectacles out of her pocket.
　　Diego lifted Scabbers out of his inside pocket and placed him next to the cage of his fellow rats, who stopped their skipping tricks and scuffled to the wire for a better took.
　　Like nearly everything Diego owned, Scabbers the rat was secondhand (he had once belonged to Diego's brother Miguel) and a bit battered. Next to the glossy rats in the cage, he looked especially woebegone.
　　"Hm," said the witch, picking up Scabbers. "How old is this rat?"
　　"Dunno," said Diego. "Quite old. He used to belong to my brother."
　　"What powers does he have?" said the witch, examining Scabbers closely.
　　"Er --" The truth was that Scabbers had never shown the faintest trace of interesting powers. The witchs eyes moved from Scabbers's tattered left ear to his front paw, which had a toe missing, and tutted loudly.
　　"He's been through the mill, this one," she said.
　　"He was like that when Miguel gave him to me," said Diego defensively.
　　"An ordinary common or garden rat like this can't be expected to live longer than three years or so," said the witch. "Now, if you were looking for something a bit more hard-wearing, you might like one of these --"
　　She indicated the black rats, who promptly started skipping again. Diego muttered, "Show-offs."
　　"Well, if you Don't want a replacement, you can try this rat tonic," said the witch, reaching under the counter and bringing out a small red bottle.
　　"Okay," said Diego. "How much -- OUCH!"
　　Diego buckled as something huge and orange came soaring from the top of the highest cage, landed on his head, and then propelled itself, spitting madly, at Scabbers.
　　"NO, CROOKSHANKS, NO!" cried the witch, but Scabbers, shot from between her hands like a bar of soap, landed splay-legged on the floor, and then scampered for the door.
　　"Scabbers!" Diego shouted, racing out of the shop after him; Roberto followed.
　　It took them nearly ten minutes to catch Scabbers, who had taken refuge under a wastepaper bin outside Quality Quidditch Supplies. Diego stuffed the trembling rat back into his pocket and straightened up, massaging his head.
　　"What was that?"
　　"It was either a very big cat or quite a small tiger," said Roberto.
　　"Where's Carolina?"
　　"Probably getting her owl
　　They made their way back up the crowded street to the Magical Menagerie. As they reached it, Carolina came out, but she wasn't carrying an owl. Her arms were clamped tightly around the enormous ginger cat.
　　"You bought that monster?" said Diego, his mouth hanging open.
　　"He's gorgeous, isn't he?" said Carolina, glowing.
　　That was a matter of opinion, thought Roberto. The cat's ginger fur was thick and fluffy, but it was definitely a bit bowlegged and its face looked grumpy and oddly squashed, as though it had run headlong into a brick wall. Now that Scabbers was out of sight, however, the cat was purring contentedly in Carolina's arms.
　　"Herinione, that thing nearly scalped me!" said Diego.
　　"He didn't mean to, did you, Crookshanks?" said Carolina.
　　"And what about Scabbers?" said Diego, pointing at the lump in his chest pocket. "He needs rest and relaxation! How's he going to get it with that thing around?"
　　"That reminds me, you forgot your rat tonic," said Carolina, slapping the small red bottle into Diego's hand. "And stop worrying, Crookshanks will be sleeping in my dormitory and Scabbers in yours, what's the problem? Poor Crookshanks, that witch said he'd been in there for ages; no one wanted him."
　　"Wonder why," said Diego sarcastically as they set off toward the Leaky Cauldron.
　　They found Mr. Lopez sitting in the bar, reading the Daily prophet.
　　"Roberto!" he said, smiling as he looked up. "How are you?"
　　"Fine, thanks," said Roberto as he, Diego, and Carolina joined Mr. Lopez with A their shopping.
　　Mr. Lopez put down his paper, and Roberto saw the now familiar picture of Gustavo Martinez staring up at him.
　　"They still haven't caught him, then?" he asked.
　　"No," said Mr. Lopez, looking extremely grave. "They've pulled us all off our regular jobs at the Ministry to try and find him, but no luck so far."
　　"Would we get a reward if we caught him?" asked Diego. "It'd be good to get some more money --"
　　"Don't be ridiculous, Diego," said Mr. Lopez, who on closer inspection looked very strained. "Martinez's not going to be caught by a thirteen-year-old wizard. It's the Azkaban guards who'll get him back, You mark my words."
　　At that moment Mrs. Lopez entered the bar, laden with shopping bags and followed by the twins, Pablo and Luis, who were about to start their fifth year at Hogwarts; the newly elected Head Boy, Miguel; and the Lopezs' youngest child and only girl, Martina.
　　Martina, who had always been very taken with Roberto, seemed even more heartily embarrassed than usual when she saw him, perhaps because he had saved her life during their previous year at Hogwarts. She went very red and muttered "hello" without looking at him. Miguel, however, held out his hand solemnly as though he and Roberto had never met and said, "Roberto. How nice to see you.
　　"Hello, Miguel," said Roberto, trying not to laugh.
　　I hope you're well?" said Miguel pompously, shaking hands. It was rather like being introduced to the mayor.
　　"Very well, thanks --"
　　"Roberto!" said Pablo, elbowing Miguel out of the way and bowing deeply. "Simply splendid to see you, old boy --"
　　"Marvelous," said Luis, pushing Pablo aside and seizing Roberto's hand in turn. "Absolutely spiffing."
　　Miguel scowled.
　　"That's enough, now," said Mrs. Lopez.
　　"Mum!" said Pablo as though he'd only just spotted her and seizing her hand too. "How really corking to see you --"
　　"I said, that's enough," said Mrs. Lopez, depositing her shopping in an empty chair. "Hello, Roberto, dear. I suppose you've heard our exciting news?" She pointed to the brand-new silver badge on Miguel's chest. "Second Head Boy in the family!" she said, swelling with pride.
　　"And last," Pablo muttered under his breath.
　　I don't doubt that," said Mrs. Lopez, frowning suddenly. "I notice they haven't made you two prefects."
　　"What do we want to be prefects for?" said Luis, looking revolted at the very idea. "It'd take all the fun out of life."
　　Martina giggled.
　　"Yo u want to set a better example for your sister!" snapped Mrs. Lopez.
　　"Martina's got other brothers to set her an example, Mother," said Miguel loftily. "I'm going up to change for dinner..."
　　He disappeared and Luis heaved a sigh.
　　"We tried to shut him in a pyramid," he told Roberto. "But Mum spotted us."
　　Dinner that night was a very enjoyable affair. Oscar the innkeeper put three tables together in the parlor, and the seven Lopezs, Roberto, and Carolina ate their way through five delicious courses.
　　"How're we getting to King's Cross tomorrow, Dad?" asked Pablo as they dug into a sumptuous chocolate pudding.
　　"The Ministry's providing a couple of cars," said Mr. Lopez.
　　Everyone looked up at him.
　　"Why?" said Miguel curiously.
　　"It's because of you, Perce," said Luis seriously. "And there'll be little flags on the hoods, with HB on them"
　　"-- for Humongous Bighead," said Pablo.
　　Everyone except Miguel and Mrs. Lopez snorted into their pudding.
　　"Why are the Ministry providing cars, Father?" Miguel asked again, in a dignified voice.
　　"Well, as we haven't got one anymore," said Mr. Lopez,
　　"-- and as I work there, they're doing me a favor --"
　　His voice was casual, but Roberto couldn't help noticing that Mr. Lopez's ears had gone red, just like Diego's did when he was under Pressure.
　　"Good thing, too," said Mrs. Lopez briskly. "Do you realize how much luggage you've all got between you? A nice sight you'd be on the Muggle Underground.... You are all packed, aren't you?"
　　"Diego hasn't put all his new things in his trunk yet," said Miguel, in a long-suffering voice. "He's dumped them on my bed."
　　"You'd better go and pack properly, Diego, because we won't have much time in the morning," Mrs. Lopez called down the table. Diego scowled at Miguel.
　　After dinner everyone felt very full and sleepy. One by one they made their way upstairs to their rooms to check their things for the next day. Diego and Miguel were next door to Roberto. He had just closed and locked his own trunk when he heard angry voices through the wall, and went to see what was going on.
　　The door of number twelve was ajar and Miguel was shouting.
　　"It was here, on the bedside table, I took it off for polishing
　　"I haven't touched it, all right?" Diego roared back.
　　"What's up?" said Roberto.
　　"My Head Boy badge is gone," said Miguel, rounding on Roberto.
　　"So's Scabbers's rat tonic," said Diego, throwing things out of his trunk to look. "I think I might've left it in the bar --"
　　"You're not going anywhere till you've found my badge!" yelled Miguel.
　　"I'll get Scabbers's stuff, I'm packed," Roberto said to Diego, and he went downstairs.
　　Roberto was halfway along the passage to the bar, which was now very dark, when he heard another pair of angry voices coming from the parlor. A second later, he recognized them as Mr. and Mrs.
　　Lopezs'. He hesitated, not wanting them to know he'd heard them arguing, when the sound of his own name made him stop, then move closer to the parlor door.
　　"--makes no sense not to tell him," Mr. Lopez was saying heatedly. "Roberto's got a right to know. I've tried to tell Fudge, but he insists on treating Roberto like a child. He's thirteen years old and --"
　　"Raul, the truth would terrify him!" said Mrs. Lopez shrilly. "Do you really want to send Roberto back to school with that hanging over him? For heaven's sake, he's happy not knowing!"
　　"I don't want to make him miserable, I want to put him on his guard!" retorted Mr. Lopez. "You know what Roberto and Diego are like, wandering off by themselves -- they've ended up in the Forbidden Forest twice! But Roberto mustn't do that this year! When I think what could have happened to him that night he ran away from home! If the Knight Bus hadn't picked him up, I'm prepared to bet he would have been dead before the Ministry found him."
　　"But he's not dead, he's fine, so what's the point
　　"Alicia, they say Gustavo Martinez's mad, and maybe he is, but he was clever enough to escape from Azkaban, and that's supposed to be impossible. It's been three weeks, and no one's seen hide nor hair of him, and I don't care what Fudge keeps telling the Daily Prophet, we're no nearer catching Martinez than inventing self-spelling wands. The only thing we know for sure is what Martinez's after
　　"But Roberto will be perfectly safe at Hogwarts."
　　"We thought Azkaban was perfectly safe. If Martinez can break out of Azkaban, he can break into Hogwarts."
　　"But no one's really sure that Martinez's after Roberto
　　There was a thud on wood, and Roberto was sure Mr. Lopez had banged his fist on the table.

The guards told Fudge that Martinezs been talking in his sleep for a while now. Always the same words: 'He's at Hogwarts... he's at Hogwarts.' Martinez is deranged, Alicia, and he wants Roberto dead. If you ask me, he thinks murdering Roberto will bring You-Know-Who back to pow er. Martinez lost everything the night Roberto stopped You- Know-Who, and he's had twelve years alone in Azkaban to brood on that...."
　　There was a silence. Roberto leaned still closer to the door, desperate to hear more.
　　"Well, Raul, you must do what you think is right. But you're forgetting Alfonso Dominguez. I don't think anything could hurt Roberto at Hogwarts while Dominguez's headmaster. I suppose he knows about all this?"
　　"Of course he knows. We had to ask him if he minds the Azkaban guards stationing themselves around the entrances to the school grounds. He wasn't happy about it, but he agreed."
　　"Not happy? Why shouldn't he be happy, if they're there to catch Martinez?"
　　"Dominguez isn't fond of the Azkaban guards," said Mr. Lopez heavily. "Nor am 1, if it comes to that... but when you're dealing with a wizard like Martinez, you sometimes have to join forces with those you'd rather avoid."
　　"If they save Roberto then I will never say another word against them, said Mr. Lopez wearily. "It's late, Alicia, we'd better go up...."
　　Roberto heard chairs move. As quietly as he could, he hurried down the passage to the bar and out of sight. The parlor door opened, and a few seconds later footsteps told him that Mr. and Mrs. Lopez were climbing the stairs.
　　The bottle of rat tonic was lying under the table they had sat at earlier. Roberto waited until he heard Mr. and Mrs. Lopez's bedroom door close, then headed back upstairs with the bottle.
　　Pablo and Luis were crouching in the shadows on the landing, heaving with laughter as they listened to Miguel dismantling his and Diego's room in search of his badge.
　　"We've got it," Pablo whispered to Roberto. "We've been improving it."
　　The badge now read Bighead Boy.
　　Roberto forced a laugh, went to give Diego the rat tonic, then shut himself in his room and lay down on his bed.
　　So Gustavo Martinez was after him. This explained everything. Fudge had been lenient with him because he was so relieved to find him alive. He'd made Roberto promise to stay in Diagon Alley where there were plenty of wizards to keep an eye on him. And he was sending two Ministry cars to take them all to the station tomorrow, so that the Lopezs could look after Roberto until he was on the train.
　　Roberto lay listening to the muffled shouting next door and wondered why he didn't feel more scared. Gustavo Martinez had murdered thirteen people with one curse; Mr. and Mrs, Lopez obviously thought Roberto would be panic-stricken if he knew the truth. But Roberto happened to agree wholeheartedly with Mrs. Lopez that the safest place on earth was wherever Alfonso Dominguez happened to be. Didn't people always say that Dominguez was the only person Lord Salvador had ever been afraid of? Surely Martinez, as Salvador's right-hand man, would be just as frightened of him?
　　And then there were these Azkaban guards everyone kept talking about. They seemed to scare most people senseless, and if they were stationed all around the school, Martinez's chances of getting inside seemed very remote.
　　No, all in all, the thing that bothered Roberto most was the fact that his chances of visiting Hogsmeade now looked like zero. Nobody would want Roberto to leave the safety of the castle until Martinez was caught; in fact, Roberto suspected his every move would be carefully watched until the danger had passed.
　　He scowled at the dark ceiling. Did they think he couldn't look after himself? He'd escaped Lord Salvador three times; he wasn't completely useless....
　　Unbidden, the image of the beast in the shadows of Magnolia Crescent crossed his mind. What to do when you know the worst is coming...
　　"I'm not going to be murdered," Roberto said out loud.
　　"That's the spirit, dear," said his mirror sleepily.
　　CHAPTER FIVE
　　THE DEMENTOR
　　Oscar woke Roberto the next morning with his usual toothless grin and a cup of tea. Roberto got dressed and was just persuading a disgruntled Hedwig to get back into her cage when Diego banged his way into the room, pulling a sweatshirt over his head and looking irritable.
　　"The sooner we get on the train, the better," he said. "At least I can get away from Miguel at Hogwarts. Now he's accusing me of dripping tea on his photo of Penelope Clearwater. You know," Diego grimaced, "his girlfriend. She's hidden her face under the frame because her nose has gone all blotchy..."
　　"I've got something to tell you," Roberto began, but they were interrupted by Pablo and Luis, who had looked in to congratulate Diego on infuriating Miguel again.
　　They headed down to breakfast, where Mr. Lopez was reading the front page of the Daily Prophet with a furrowed brow and Mrs. Lopez was telling Carolina and Martina about a love potion she'd made as a young girl. All three of them were rather giggly.
　　"What were you saying?" Diego asked Roberto as they sat down.
　　"Later," Roberto muttered as Miguel stormed in.
　　Roberto had no chance to speak to Diego or Carolina in the chaos of leaving; they were too busy heaving all their trunks down the Leaky Cauldron's narrow staircase and piling them up near the door, with Hedwig and Hermes, Miguel's screech owl, perched on top in their cages. A small wickerwork basket stood beside the heap of trunks, spitting loudly.
　　"It's all right, Crookshanks," Carolina cooed through the wickerwork. "I'll let you out on the train."
　　"You won't," snapped Diego. "What about poor Scabbers, eh?"
　　He pointed at his chest, where a large lump indicated that Scabbers was curled up in his pocket.
　　Mr. Lopez, who had been outside waiting for the Ministry cars, stuck his head inside.
　　"They're here, he said. "Roberto, come on."
　　Mr. Lopez marched Roberto across the short stretch of pavement toward the first of two old- fashioned dark green cars, each of which was driven by a furtive-looking wizard wearing a suit of emerald velvet.
　　"In you get, Roberto," said Mr. Lopez, glancing up and down the crowded street.
　　Roberto got into the back of the car and was shortly joined by Carolina, Diego, and, to Diego's disgust, Miguel.
　　The journey to King's Cross was very uneventful compared with Roberto's trip on the Knight Bus. The Ministry of Magic cars seemed almost ordinary. though Roberto noticed that they could slide through gaps that Uncle Francisco's new company car certainly couldn't have managed. They reached King's Cross with twenty minutes to spare; the Ministry drivers found them trolleys, unloaded their trunks, touched their hats in salute to Mr. Lopez, and drove away, somehow managing to jump to the head of an unmoving line at the traffic lights.
　　Mr. Lopez kept close to Roberto's elbow all the way into the station.
　　"Right then," he said, glancing around them. "Let's do this in pairs, as there are so many of us. I'll go through first with Roberto."
　　Mr. Lopez strolled toward the barrier between platforms nine and ten, pushing Roberto's trolley and apparently very interested in the InterCity 125 that had just arrived at platform nine. With a meaningful look at Roberto, he leaned casually against the barrier. Roberto imitated him.
　　In a moment, they had fallen sideways through the solid metal onto platform nine and three- quarters and looked up to see the Hogwarts Express, a scarlet steam engine, puffing smoke over a platform packed with witches and wizards seeing their children onto the train.
　　Miguel and Martina suddenly appeared behind Roberto. They were panting and had apparently taken the barrier at a run.
　　"Ah, there's Penelope!" said Miguel, smoothing his hair and going Pink again. Martina caught Roberto's eye, and they both turned away to hide their laughter as Miguel strode over to a girl with long, curly hair, walking with his chest thrown out so that she couldn't miss his shiny badge. stood back to let him on. They leaned out of the window and waved at Mr. and Mrs. Lopez until the train turned a corner and blocked them from view.
　　"I need to talk to you in private," Roberto muttered to Diego and Carolina as the train picked up speed.
　　"Go away, Martina," said Diego.
　　"Oh, that's nice," said Martina huffily, and she stalked off.
　　Roberto, Diego, and Carolina set off down the corridor, looking for an empty compartment, but all were full except for the one at the very end of the train.
　　This had only one occupant, a man sitting fast asleep next to the window. Roberto, Diego, and Carolina checked on the threshold. The Hogwarts Express was usually reserved for students and they had never seen an adult there before, except for the witch who pushed the food cart.
　　The stranger was wearing an extremely shabby set of wizard's robes that had been darned in several places. He looked ill and exhausted. Though quite young, his light brown hair was flecked with gray.
　　"Who d'you reckon he is?" Diego hissed as they sat down and slid the door shut, taking the seats farthest away from the window.
　　"Professor R. J. Cardozo," whispered Carolina at once.
　　"How d'you know that?"
　　"It's on his case," she replied, pointing at the luggage rack over the man's head, where there was a small, battered case held together with a large quantity of neatly knotted string. The name Professor R. J. Cardozo was stamped across one corner in peeling letters.
　　"Wonder what he teaches?" said Diego, frowning at Professor Cardozo's pallid profile.
　　"That's obvious," whispered Carolina. "There's only one vacancy, isn't there? Defense Against the Dark Arts."
　　Roberto, Diego, and Carolina had already had two Defense Against the Dark Arts teachers, both of whom had lasted only one year. There were rumors that the job was jinxed.
　　"well, I hope he's up to it," said Diego doubtfully. "He looks like on, good hex would finish him off, doesn't he? Anyway..." He turned to Roberto. "What were you going to tell us?"
　　Roberto explained all about Mr. and Mrs. Lopez's argument and the warning Mr. Lopez had just given him. \When he'd finished, Diego looked thunderstruck, and Carolina had her hands over her mouth. She finally lowered them to say, "Gustavo Martinez escaped to come after you? Oh, Roberto... you'll have to be really, really careful. don't go looking for trouble, Roberto --"
　　"I Don't go looking for trouble," said Roberto, nettled. "Trouble usually finds me."
　　"How thick would Roberto have to be, to go looking for a nutter who wants to kill him?" said Diego shakily.
　　They were taking the news worse than Roberto had expected. Both Diego and Carolina seemed to be much more frightened of Martinez than he was.
　　"No one knows how he got out of Azkaban," said Diego uncomfortably. "No one's ever done it before. And he was a top-security prisoner too."
　　"But they'll catch him, won't they?" said Carolina earnestly. "I Mean, they've got all the Muggles looking out for him too...." "What's that noise?" said Diego suddenly.
　　A faint, tinny sort of whistle was coming from somewhere. The, looked all around the compartment.
　　"It's coming from your trunk, Roberto," said Diego, standing UP and reaching into the luggage rack. A moment later he had pulled the Pocket Sneakoscope out from between Roberto's robes. It was spinning very fast in the palm of Diego's hand and glowing brilliantly.
　　"Is that a Sneakoscope?" said Carolina interestedly, standing up for a better look.
　　"Yeah... mind you, it's a very cheap one," Diego said. "It went haywire just as I was tying it to Errol's leg to send it to Roberto."
　　"Were you doing anything untrustworthy at the time?" said Carolina shrewdly.

　　"We could get it checked in Hogsmeade," said Diego, sitting back down. "They sell that sort of thing in Dervish and Banges, magical instruments and stuff. Pablo and Luis told me."
　　"Do you know much about Hogsmeade?" asked Carolina keenly. "I've read it's the only entirely non-Muggle settlement in Britain --"

Pepper Imps -- they make you smoke at the mouth -- and great fat Paulacoballs full of strawberry mousse and clotted cream, and really excellent sugar quills, which you can suck in class and just look like you're thinking what to write next --"
　　"But Hogsmeade's a very interesting place, isn't it?" Carolina pressed on eagerly. "In Sites of Historical Sorcery it says the inn was the headquarters for the 1612 goblin rebellion, and the Shrieking Shades supposed to be the most severely haunted building in Britain --"
　　"-- and massive sherbert balls that make you levitate a few inches off the ground while you're sucking them," said Diego, who was plainly not listening to a word Carolina was saying.
　　Carolina looked around at Roberto.
　　"Won't it be nice to get out of school for a bit and explore Hogsmeade?"
　　"'Spect it will," said Roberto heavily. "You'll have to tell me when You've found out."

　　"I can't go. The Carrizos didn't sign my permission form, and Fudge wouldn't either."
　　Diego looked horrified.
　　""You're not allowed to come? But -- no way -- Romero or someone will give you permission -- " musclely; Crabbe was taller, with a pudding-bowl haircut and a very thick neck; Goyle had short, bristly hair and long, gorilla-ish arms.

　　Sosa's pale eyes narrowed; he wasn't fool enough to pick a fight right under a teacher's nose.

　　Roberto and Diego sat down again, Diego massaging his knuckles.

　　Diego made a violent gesture in midair.
　　"Diego," hissed Carolina, pointing at Professor Cardozo, "be careful..."
　　But Professor Cardozo was still fast asleep.
　　The rain thickened as the train sped yet farther north; the windows were now a solid, shimmering gray, which graduily darkened until lanterns flickered into life all along the corridors and over the luggage racks. The train rattled, the rain hammered, the ind roared, but still, Professor Cardozo slept.
　　"We must be nearly there," said Diego, leaning forward to look past Professor Cardozo at the now completely black window.
　　The words had hardly left him when the train started to slow down.
　　"Great," said Diego, getting up and walking carefully past Professor Cardozo to try and see outside. "I'm starving. I want to get to the feast....
　　"We can't be there yet," said Carolina, checking her watch.
　　"So why're we stopping?"
　　The train was getting slower and slower. As the noise of the pistons fell away, the wind and rain sounded louder than ever against the windows.
　　Roberto, who was nearest the door, got up to look into the corridor. All along the carriage, heads were sticking curiously out of their compartments.
　　The train came to a stop with a jolt, and distant thuds and bangs told them that luggage had fallen out of the racks. Then, without warning, all the lamps went out and they were plunged into total darkness.
　　"'What's going on?" said Diego's voice from behind Roberto.
　　"Ouch!" gasped Carolina. "Diego, that was my foot!"
　　Roberto felt his way back to his seat.
　　"D'you think we've broken down?"
　　"Dunno..."
　　There was a squeaking sound, and Roberto saw the dim black outline of Diego, wiping a patch clean on the window and peering out.
　　"There's something moving out there," Diego said. "I think people are coming aboard...."
　　The compartment door suddenly opened and someone fell painfully over Roberto's legs.
　　"Sorry -- d'you know what's going on? -- Ouch -- sorry
　　"Hullo, Mauricio," said Roberto, feeling around in the dark and pulling Mauricio up by his cloak.
　　"Roberto? Is that you? What's happening?"
　　"No idea -- sit down --"
　　There was a loud hissing and a yelp of pain; Mauricio had tried to sit on Crookshanks.
　　"I'm going to go and ask the driver what's going on," came Carolina's voice. Roberto felt her pass him, heard the door slide open again, and then a thud and two loud squeals of pain.
　　"Who's that?"
　　"Who's that?"
　　"Martina?"
　　"Carolina?"
　　"What are you doing?"
　　"I was looking for Diego --" "Come in and sit down --"
　　"Not here!" said Roberto hurriedly. "I'm here!"
　　"Ouch!" said Mauricio.
　　"Quiet!" said a hoarse voice suddenly.
　　Professor Cardozo appeared to have woken up at last. Roberto could hear movements in his corner.
　　None of them spoke.
　　There was a soft, crackling noise, and a shivering light filled the compartment. Professor Cardozo appeared to be holding a handful of flames. They illuminated his tired, gray face, but his eyes looked alert and wary.
　　"Stay where you are," he said in the same hoarse voice, and he got slowly to his feet with his handful of fire held out in front of him.
　　But the door slid slowly open before Cardozo could reach it.
　　Standing in the doorway, illuminated by the shivering flames in Cardozo's hand, was a cloaked figure that towered to the ceiling. Its face was completely hidden beneath its hood. Roberto's eyes darted downward, and what he saw made his stomach contract. There was a hand protruding from the cloak and it was glistening, grayish, slimy-looking, and scabbed, like something dead that had decayed in water...
　　But it was visible only for a split second. As though the creature beneath the cloak sensed Roberto's gaze, the hand was suddenly withdrawn into the folds of its black cloak.
　　And then the thing beneath the hood, whatever it was, drew a long, slow, rattling breath, as though it were trying to suck something more than air from its surroundings.
　　An intense cold swept over them all. Roberto felt his own breath catch in his chest. The cold went deeper than his skin. It was inside his chest, it was inside his very heart....
　　Roberto's eyes rolled up into his head. He couldn't see. He was drowning in cold. There was a rushing in his ears as though of water. He was being dragged downward, the roaring growing louder. .
　　And then, from far away, he heard screaming, terrible, terrified, pleading screams. He wanted to help whoever it was, he tried to move his arms, but couldn't... a thick white fog was swirling around him, inside him -
　　"Roberto! Roberto! Are you all right?"
　　Someone was slapping his face.
　　"W -- what?"
　　Roberto opened his eyes; there were lanterns above him, and the floor was shaking -- the Hogwarts Express was moving again and the lights had come back on. He seemed to have slid out of his seat onto the floor. Diego and Carolina were kneeling next to him, and above them he could see Mauricio and Professor Cardozo watching. Roberto felt very sick; when he put up his hand to push his glasses back on, he felt cold sweat on his face.
　　Diego and Carolina heaved him back onto his seat.
　　"Are you okay?" Diego asked nervously.
　　"Yeah," said Roberto, looking quickly toward the door. The hooded creature had vanished. "What happened? Where's that -- that thing? Who screamed?"
　　"No one screamed," said Diego, more nervously still.
　　Roberto looked around the bright compartment. Martina and Mauricio looked back at him, both very pale.
　　"But I heard screaming --"
　　A loud snap made them all jump. Professor Cardozo was breaking an enormous slab of chocolate into pieces.
　　"Here," he said to Roberto, handing him a particularly large piece. "Eat it. It'll help."
　　Roberto took the chocolate but didn't eat it.
　　"What was that thing?" he asked Cardozo.
　　"A dementor," said Cardozo, who was now giving chocolate to everyone else. "One of the dementors of Azkaban."
　　Everyone stared at him. Professor Cardozo crumpled up the empty chocolate wrapper and put it in his pocket.
　　"Eat," he repeated. "It'll help. I need to speak to the driver, excuse me...
　　He strolled past Roberto and disappeared into the corridor.
　　"Are you sure you're okay, Roberto?" said Carolina, watching Roberto anxiously.
　　"I Don't get it.... What happened?" said Roberto, wiping more sweat off his face.
　　"Well -- that thing -- the dementor -- stood there and looked around (I mean, I think it did, I couldn't see its face) -- and you -- you
　　"I thought you were having a fit or something," said Diego, who still looked scared. "You went sort of rigid and fell out of your seat and started twitching -- 11
　　"And Professor Cardozo stepped over you, and walked toward the dementor, and pulled out his wand," said Carolina, "and he said, 'None of us is hiding Gustavo Martinez under our cloaks. Go.' But the dementor didn't move, so Cardozo muttered something, and a silvery thing shot out of his wand at it, and it turned around and sort of glided away.... "
　　"It was horrible," said Mauricio, in a higher voice than usual. "Did YOU feel how cold it got when it came in?"
　　I felt weird," said Diego, shifting his shoulders uncomfortably. "Like I'd never be cheerful again...."
　　Martina, who was huddled in her corner looking nearly as bad as Roberto felt, gave a small sob; Carolina went over and put a comforting arm around her.
　　"But didn't any of you -- fall off your seats?" said Roberto awkwardly.
　　"No," said Diego, looking anxiously at Roberto again. "Martina was shaking like mad, though...."
　　Roberto didn't understand. He felt weak and shivery, as though he were recovering from a bad bout of flu; he also felt the beginnings of shame. Why had he gone to pieces like that, when no one else had?
　　Professor Cardozo had come back. He paused as he entered, looked around, and said, with a small smile, "I haven't poisoned that chocolate, you know...."
　　Roberto took a bite and to his great surprise felt warmth spread suddenly to the tips of his fingers and toes.
　　"We'll be at Hogwarts in ten minutes," said Professor Cardozo. "Are you all right, Roberto?"
　　Roberto didn't ask how Professor Cardozo knew his name.
　　"Fine," he muttered, embarrassed.
　　They didn't talk much during the remainder of the journey. At long last, the train stopped at Hogsmeade station, and there was a great scramble to get outside; owls hooted, cats meowed, and Mauricio's pet toad croaked loudly from under his hat. It was freezing on the tiny platform; rain was driving down in icy sheets.
　　"Firs' years this way!" called a familiar voice. Roberto, Diego, and Carolina turned and saw the gigantic outline of Villalba at the other end of the platform, beckoning the terrified-looking new students forward for their traditional journey across the lake.
　　"All right, you three?" Villalba yelled over the heads of the crowd. They waved at him, but had no chance to speak to him because the mass of people around them was shunting them away along the platform. Roberto, Diego, and Carolina followed the rest of the school along the platform and out onto a rough mud track, where at least a hundred stagecoaches awaited the remaining students, each pulled, Roberto could only assume, by an invisible horse, because when they climbed inside and shut the door, the coach set off all by itself, bumping and swaying in procession.
　　The coach smelled faintly of mold and straw. Roberto felt better since the chocolate, but still weak. Diego and Carolina kept looking at him sideways, as though frightened he might collapse again.
　　As the carriage trundled toward a pair of magnificent wrought iron gates, flanked with stone columns topped with winged boars,
　　Roberto saw two more towering, hooded dementors, standing guard on either side. A wave of cold sickness threatened to engulf him again; he leaned back into the lumpy seat and closed his eyes until they had passed the gates. The carriage picked up speed on the long, sloping drive up to the castle; Carolina was leaning out of the tiny window, watching the many turrets and towers draw nearer. At last, the carriage swayed to a halt, and Carolina and Diego got out.
　　As Roberto stepped down, a drawling, delighted voice sounded in his ear.
　　"You fainted, Gonzales? Is Longbottorn telling the truth? You actualy fainted?"
　　Sosa elbowed past Carolina to block Roberto's way up the stone steps to the castle, his face gleeful and his pale eyes glinting maliciously. "Shove off, Sosa," said Diego, whose jaw was clenched.
　　"Did you faint as well, Lopez?" said Sosa loudly. "Did the scary old dementor frighten you too, Lopez?"
　　"Is there a problem?" said a mild voice. Professor Cardozo had just gotten out of the next carriage.
　　Sosa gave Professor Cardozo an insolent stare, which took in the patches on his robes and the delapidated suitcase. With a tiny hint of sarcasm in his voice, he said, "Oh, no -- er -- Professor," then he smirked at Crabbe and Goyle and led them up the steps into the castle.
　　Carolina prodded Diego in the back to make him hurry, and the three of them joined the crowd swarming up the steps, through the giant oak front doors, into the cavernous entrance hall, which was lit with flaming torches, and housed a magnificent marble staircase that led to the upper floors.
　　The door into the Great Hall stood open at the right; Roberto followed the crowd toward it, but had barely glimpsed the enchanted ceiling, which was black and cloudy tonight, when a voice called, "Gonzales! Fernandez! I want to see you both!"
　　Roberto and Carolina turned around, surprised. Professor Romero, Transfiguration teacher and head of Gryffindor House, was calling over the heads of the crowd. She was a sternlooking witch who wore her hair in a tight bun; her sharp eyes were framed with square spectacles. Roberto fought his way over to her with a feeling of foreboding: Professor Romero had a way of making him feel he must have done something wrong.
　　"There's no need to look so worried -- I just want a word in MY office," she told them. "Move along there, Lopez."
　　Diego stared as Professor Romero ushered Roberto and Carolina away from the chattering crowd; they accompanied her across the entrance hall, up the marble staircase, and along a corridor.
　　Once they were in her office, a small room with a large, welcoming fire, Professor Romero motioned Roberto and Carolina to sit down. She settled herself behind her desk and said abruptly, "Professor Cardozo sent an owl ahead to say that you were taken ill on the train, Gonzales."
　　Before Roberto could reply, there was a soft knock on the door and Madam Pomfrey, the nurse, came bustling in.
　　Roberto felt himself going red in the face. It was bad enough that he'd passed out, or whatever he had done, without everyone making all this fuss.
　　"I'm fine," he said, "I don't need anything
　　"Oh, it's you, is it?" said Madam Pomfrey, ignoring this and bending down to stare closely at him. "I suppose you've been doing something dangerous again?"
　　"It was a dementor, Poppy," said Professor Romero.
　　They exchanged a dark look, and Madam Pomfrey clucked disapprovingly.
　　"Setting dementors around a school, she muttered, pushing back Roberto's hair and feeling his forehead. "He won't be the last one who collapses. Yes, he's all clammy. Terrible things, they are, and the effect they have on people who are already delicate
　　"I'm not delicate!" said Roberto crossly.
　　"Of course you're not," said Madam Pomfrey absentmindedly, now taking his pulse.
　　"What does he need?" said Professor Romero crisply. "Bed rest? Should he perhaps spend tonight in the hospital wing?"
　　"I'm fine!" said Roberto, jumping up. The thought of what Javier Sosa would say if he had to go to the hospital wing was torture.
　　"Well, he should have some chocolate, at the very least," said Madam Pomfrey, who was now trying to peer into Roberto's eyes.
　　"I've already had some," said Roberto. "Professor Cardozo gave me some. He gave it to all of us."
　　"Did he, now?" said Madam Pomfrey approvingly. "So we've finally got a Defense Against the Dark Arts teacher who knows his remedies?"
　　"Are you sure you feel all right, Gonzales?" Professor Romero said sharply.
　　"Yes, "said Roberto.
　　"Very well. Kindly wait outside while I have a quick word with Miss Fernandez about her course schedule, then we can go down to the feast together."
　　Roberto went back into the corridor with Madam Pomfrey, who left for the hospital wing, muttering to herself He had to wait only a few minutes; then Carolina emerged looking very happy about something, followed by Professor Romero, and the three of them made their way back down the marble staircase to the Great Hall.
　　It was a sea of pointed black hats; each of the long House tables was lined with students, their faces glimmering by the light of thousands of candles, which were floating over the tables in midair. Professor Flitwick, who was a tiny little wizard with a shock of white hair, was carrying an ancient hat and a three-legged stool out of the hall.
　　"Oh," said Carolina softly, "we've missed the Sorting!"
　　New students at Hogwarts were sorted into Houses by trying on the sorting Hat, which shouted out the House they were best suited to (Gryffindor, Ravenclaw, Hufflepuff, or Slytherin). Professor Romero strode off toward her empty seat at the staff table, and Roberto and Carolina set off in the other direction, as quietly as possible, toward the Gryffindor table. People looked around at them as they passed along the back of the hall, and a few of them pointed at Roberto. Had the story of his collapsing in front of the dementor traveled that fast?
　　He and Carolina sat down on either side of Diego, who had saved them seats.
　　"What was all that about?" he muttered to Roberto.
　　Roberto started to explain in a whisper, but at that moment the headmaster stood up to speak, and he broke off.
　　Professor Dominguez, though very old, always gave an impression of great energy. He had several feet of long silver hair and beard, half-moon spectacles, and an extremely crooked nose. He was often described as the greatest wizard of the age, but that wasn't why Roberto respected him. You couldn't help trusting Alfonso Dominguez, and as Roberto watched him beaming around at the students, he felt really calm for the first time since the dementor had entered the train compartment.
　　"Welcome!" said Dominguez, the candlelight shimmering on his beard. "Welcome to another year at Hogwarts! I have a few things to say to you all, and as one of them is very serious, I think it best to get it out of the way before you become befuddled by our excellent feast...."
　　Dominguez cleared his throat and continued, "As you will all be aware after their search of the Hogwarts Express, our school is presently playing host to some of the dementors of Azkaban, who are here on Ministry of Magic business."
　　He paused, and Roberto remembered what Mr. Lopez had said about Dominguez not being happy with the dementors guarding the school.
　　"They are stationed at every entrance to the grounds," Dominguez continued, "and while they are with us, I must make it plain that nobody is to leave school without permission. Dementors are not to be fooled by tricks or disguises -- or even Invisibility Cloaks," he added blandly, and Roberto and Diego glanced at each other. "It is not in the nature of a dementor to understand pleading or excuses. I therefore warn each and every one of you to give them no reason to harm you. I look to the prefects, and our new Head Boy and Girl, to make sure that no student runs afoul of the dementors," he said.
　　Miguel, who was sitting a few seats down from Roberto, puffed out his chest again and stared around impressively. Dominguez paused again; he looked very seriously around the hall, and nobody moved or made a sound.
　　"On a happier note," he continued, I am pleased to welcome two new teachers to our ranks this year.
　　"First, Professor Cardozo, who has kindly consented to fill the post of Defense Against the Dark Arts teacher."
　　There was some scattered, rather unenthusiastic applause. Only those who had been in the compartment on the train with Professor Cardozo clapped hard, Roberto among them. Professor Cardozo looked particularly shabby next to all the other teachers in their best robes.
　　"Look at Peralta!" Diego hissed in Roberto's ear.
　　Professor Peralta, the Potions master, was staring along the staff table at Professor Cardozo. It was common knowledge that Peralta ,anted the Defense Against the Dark Arts job, but even Roberto, who hated Peralta, was startled at the expression twisting his thin, sallow face. it was beyond anger: it was loathing. Roberto knew that expression only too well; it was the look Peralta wore every time he set eyes on Roberto.
　　"As to our second new appointment," Dominguez continued as the lukewarm applause for Professor Cardozo died away. "Well, I am sorry to tell you that Professor Kettleburn, our Care of Magical Creatures teacher, retired at the end of last year in order to enjoy more time with his remaining limbs. However, I am delighted to say that his place will be filled by none other than Matias Villalba, who has agreed to take on this teaching job in addition to his gamekeeping duties."
　　Roberto, Diego, and Carolina stared at one another, stunned. Then they joined in with the applause, which was tumultuous at the Gryffindor table in particular. Roberto leaned forward to see Villalba, who was ruby-red in the face and staring down at his enormous hands, his wide grin hidden in the tangle of his black beard.
　　"We should've known!" Diego roared, pounding the table. "Who else would have assigned us a biting book?"
　　Roberto, Diego, and Carolina were the last to stop clapping, and as Professor Dominguez started speaking again, they saw that Villalba was wiping his eyes on the tablecloth.
　　"Well, I think that's everything of importance," said Dominguez. "Let the feast begin!"
　　The golden plates and goblets before them filled suddenly with food and drink. Roberto, suddenly ravenous, helped himself to everything he could reach and began to eat.
　　It was a delicious feast; the hall echoed with talk, laughter, and the clatter of knives and forks. Roberto, Diego, and Carolina, however, were eager for it to finish so that they could talk to Villalba. They knew how much being made a teacher would mean to him. Villalba wasn't a fully qualified wizard; he had been expelled from Hogwarts in his third year for a crime he had not committed. It had been Roberto, Diego, and Carolina who had cleared Villalba's name last year.
　　At long last, when the last morsels of pumpkin tart had melted from the golden platters, Dominguez gave the word that it was time for them all to go to bed, and they got their chance.
　　"Congratulations, Villalba!" Carolina squealed as they reached the teachers' table.
　　"All down ter you three," said Villalba, wiping his shining face on his napkin as he looked up at them., "Can' believe it... great man, Dominguez... came straight down to me hut after Professor Kettleburn said he'd had enough.... It's what I always wanted. --"
　　Overcome with emotion, he buried his face in his napkin, and Professor Romero shooed them away.
　　Roberto, Diego, and Carolina joined the Gryffindors streaming up the marble staircase and, very tired now, along more corridors, UP more and more stairs, to the hidden entrance to Gryffindor Tower's large portrait of a fat lady in a pink dress asked them, "Password?"
　　"Coming through, coming through!" Miguel called from behind the crowd. "The new password's 'Fortuna Major'!"
　　"Oh no," said Mauricio Silva sadly. He always had trouble remembering the passwords.
　　Through the portrait hole and across the common room, the girls and boys divided toward their separate staircases. Roberto climbed the spiral stair with no thought in his head except how glad he was to be back. They reached their familiar, circular dormitory with its five four-poster beds, and Roberto, looking around, felt he was home at last.
　　CHAPTER SIX
　　TALONS AND TEA LEAVES
　　When Roberto, Diego, and Carolina entered the Great Hall for breakfast the next day, the first thing they saw was Javier Sosa, who seemed to be entertaining a large group of Slytherins with a very funny story. As they passed, Sosa did a ridiculous impression of a swooning fit and there was a roar of laughter.
　　"Ignore him," said Carolina, who was right behind Roberto. "Just ignore him, it's not worth it...."
　　"Hey, Gonzales!" shrieked Pansy Parkinson, a Slytherin girl with a face like a pug. "Gonzales! The dementors are coming, Gonzales! Woooooooooo!"
　　Roberto dropped into a seat at the Gryffindor table, next to Luis Lopez.
　　"New third-year course schedules," said Luis, passing then, over. "What's up with you, Roberto?"
　　"Sosa," said Diego, sitting down on Luis's other side and glaring over at the Slytherin table.
　　Luis looked up in time to see Sosa pretending to faint with terror again.
　　"That little git," he said calmly. "He wasn't so cocky last night when the dementors were down at our end of the train. Came runing into our compartment, didn't he, Pablo?"
　　"Nearly wet himself," said Pablo, with a contemptuous glance at Sosa.
　　"I wasn't too happy myself," said Luis. "They're horrible things, those dementors...."
　　"Sort of freeze your insides, don't they?" said Pablo.
　　"You didn't pass out, though, did you?" said Roberto in a low voice.
　　"Forget it, Roberto," said Luis bracingly. "Dad had to go out to Azkaban one time, remember, Pablo? And he said it was the worst place he'd ever been, he came back all weak and shaking.... They suck the happiness out of a place, dementors. Most of the prisoners go mad in there."
　　"Anyway, we'll see how happy Sosa looks after our first Quidditch match," said Pablo. "Gryffindor versus Slytherin, first game of the season, remember?"
　　The only time Roberto and Sosa had faced each other in a Quidditch match, Sosa had definitely come off worse. Feeling slightly more cheerful, Roberto helped himself to sausages and fried tomatoes.
　　Carolina was examining her new schedule.
　　" Ooh, good, we're starting some new subjects today," she said happily. villains are these, that trespass upon my private lands! Come I. scorn at my fall, perchance? Draw, you knaves, you dogs!"
　　They watched in astonishment as the little knight tugged his sword out of its scabbard and began brandishing it violently, hopping up and down in rage. But the sword was too long for him; a particularly wild swing made him overbalance, and he landed facedown in the grass.
　　"Are you all right?" said Roberto, moving closer to the picture.
　　"Get back, you scurvy braggart! Back, you rogue!"
　　The knight seized his sword again and used it to push himself back up, but the blade sank deeply into the grass and, though he pulled with all his might, he couldn't get it out again. Finally, he had to flop back down onto the grass and push up his visor to mop his sweating face.
　　"Listen," said Roberto, taking advantage of the knight's exhaustion, "we're looking for the North Tower. You don't know the way, do you?"
　　"A quest!" The knight's rage seemed to vanish instantly. He clanked to his feet and shouted, "Come follow me, dear friends, and we shall find our goal, or else shall perish bravely in the charge!"
　　He gave the sword another fruitless tug, tried and failed to mount the fat pony, gave up, and cried, "On foot then, good sirs and gentle lady! On! On!"
　　And he ran, clanking loudly, into the left side of the frame and out of sight.
　　They hurried after him along the corridor, following the sound of his armor. Every now and then they spotted him running through a picture ahead.
　　"Be of stout heart, the worst is yet to come!" yelled the knight, and they saw him reappear in front of an alarmed group of women in crinolines, whose picture hung on the wall of a narrow spiral staircase.
　　Puffing loudly, Roberto, Diego, and Carolina climbed the tightly spiraling steps, getting dizzier and dizzier, until at last they heard the murmur of voices above them and knew they had reached the classroom.
　　"Farewell!" cried the knight, popping his head into a painting of some sinister-looking monks. "Farewell, my comrades-in-arms! If ever you have need of noble heart and steely sinew, call upon Sir Cadogan!"
　　"Yeah, we'll call you," muttered Diego as the knight disappeared, "if we ever need someone mental."
　　They climbed the last few steps and emerged onto a tiny landing, where most of the class was already assembled. There were no doors off this landing, but Diego nudged Roberto and pointed at the ceiling, where there was a circular trapdoor with a brass plaque on it.
　　"'Sibyll Trelawney, Divination teacher,"' Roberto read. "How're we supposed to get up there?"
　　As though in answer to his question, the trapdoor suddenly opened, and a silvery ladder descended right at Roberto's feet. Everyone got quiet.
　　"After you," said Diego, grinning, so Roberto climbed the ladder first.
　　He emerged into the strangest-looking classroom he had ever seen. In fact, it didn't look like a classroom at all, more like a cross between someone's attic and an old-fashioned tea shop. At leasttwenty small, circular tables were crammed inside it, all surrounded by chintz armchairs and fat little poufs. Everything was lit with a dim, crimson light; the curtains at the windows were all closed, and the many lamps were draped with dark red scarves. it was stiflingly warm, and the fire that was burning under the crowded mantelpiece was giving off a heavy, sickly sort of perfume as it heated a large copper kettle. The shelves running around the circular walls were crammed with dusty-looking feathers, stubs of candles, many packs of tattered playing cards, countless silvery crystal balls, and a huge array of teacups.
　　Diego appeared at Roberto's shoulder as the class assembled around them, all talking in whispers.
　　"Where is she?" Diego said.
　　A voice came suddenly out of the shadows, a soft, misty sort of voice.
　　"Welcome," it said. "How nice to see you in the physical world at last."
　　Roberto's immediate impression was of a large, glittering insect. Professor Trelawney moved into the firelight, and they saw that she was very thin; her large glasses magnified her eyes to several times their natural size, and she was draped in a gauzy spangled shawl. Innumerable chains and beads hung around her spindly neck, and her arms and hands were encrusted with bangles and rings.
　　"Sit, my children, sit," she said, and they all climbed awkwardly into armchairs or sank onto poufs. Roberto, Diego, and Carolina sat themselves around the same round table.
　　"Welcome to Divination," said Professor Trelawney, who had seated herself in a winged armchair in front of the fire. "My name is professor Trelawney. You may not have seen me before. I find that descending too often into the hustle and bustle of the main school clouds my Inner Eye."
　　Nobody said anything to this extraordinary pronouncement. Professor Trelawney delicately rearranged her shawl and continued, "So you have chosen to study Divination, the most difficult of all magical arts. I must warn you at the outset that if you do not have the Sight, there is very little I will be able to teach you.. Books can take you only so far in this field...."
　　At these words, both Roberto and Diego glanced, grinning, at Carolina, who looked startled at the news that books wouldn't be much help in this subject.
　　"Many witches and wizards, talented though they are in the area of loud bangs and smells and sudden disappearings, are yet unable to penetrate the veiled mysteries of the future," Professor Trelawney went on, her enormous, gleaming eyes moving from face to nervous face. "It is a Gift granted to few. You, boy," she said suddenly to Mauricio, who almost toppled off his pouf. "Is your grandmother well?"
　　"I think so," said Mauricio tremulously.
　　"I wouldn't be so sure if I were you, dear," said Professor Trelawney, the firelight glinting on her long emerald earrings. Mauricio gulped. Professor Trelawney continued placidly. "We will be covering the basic methods of Divination this year. The first term will be devoted to reading the tea leaves. Next term we shall progress to palmistry. By the way, my dear," she shot suddenly at Parvati Patil, "beware a red-haired man."
　　Parvati gave a startled look at Diego, who was right behind her and edged her chair away from him.
　　"In the second term," Professor Trelawney went on, "we shall progress to the crystal ball -- if we have finished with fire omens, that is. Unfortunately, classes will be disrupted in February by a nasty bout of flu. I myself will lose my voice. And around Easter, one of our number will leave us forever."
　　A very tense silence followed this pronouncement, but Professor Trelawney seemed unaware of it.
　　"I wonder, dear," she said to Lavender Brown, who was nearest and shrank back in her chair, "if you could pass me the largest silver teapot?"
　　Lavender, looking relieved, stood up, took an enormous teapot from the shelf, and put it down on the table in front of Professor Trelawney.
　　"Thank you, my dear. Incidentally, that thing you are dreading -- it will happen on Friday the sixteenth of October."
　　Lavender trembled.
　　"Now, I want you all to divide into pairs. Collect a teacup from the shelf, come to me, and I will fill it. Then sit down and drink, drink until only the dregs remain. Swill these around the cup three times with the left hand, then turn the cup upside down on its saucer, wait for the last of the tea to drain away, then give your cup to your partner to read. You will interpret the patterns using pages five and six of Unfogging the Future. I shall move among you, helping and instructing. Oh, and dear" -- she caught Mauricio by the arm as he made to stand up -- "after you've broken your first cup, would you be so kind as to select one of the blue patterned ones? I'm rather attached to the pink."
　　Sure enough, Mauricio had no sooner reached the shelf of teacups when there was a tinkle of breaking china. Professor Trelawney swept over to him holding a dustpan and brush and said, "One of the blue ones, then, dear, if you wouldn't mind... thank you. ... "
　　When Roberto and Diego had had their teacups filled, they went back to their table and tried to drink the scalding tea quickly. They swilled the dregs around as Professor Trelawney had instructed, then drained the cups and swapped over.
　　"Right," said Diego as they both opened their books at pages five and six. "What can you see in mine?"
　　"A load of soggy brown stuff," said Roberto. The heavily perfumed smoke in the room was making him feel sleepy and stupid.
　　"Broaden your minds, my dears, and allow your eyes to see past the mundane!" Professor Trelawney cried through the gloom.
　　Roberto tried to pull himself together.
　　"Right, you've got a crooked sort of cross... " He consulted Unfogging the Future. "That means you're going to have 'trials and suffering' -- sorry about that -- but there's a thing that could be the sun... hang on... that means 'great happiness'... so you're going to suffer but be very happy...."
　　"You need your Inner Eye tested, if you ask me," said Diego, and they both had to stifle their laughs as Professor Trelawney gazed in their direction.
　　"My turn..." Diego peered into Roberto's teacup, his forehead wrinkled with effort. "There's a blob a bit like a bowler hat," he said. "Maybe you're going to work for the Ministry of Magic...
　　He turned the teacup the other way up.
　　"But this way it looks more like an acorn.... What's that?" He scanned his copy of Unfogging the Future. "'A windfall, unexpected gold.' Excellent, you can lend me some... and there's a thin, here," he turned the cup again, "that looks like an animal... yeah, if that was its head... it looks like a hippo... no, a sheep..."
　　Professor Trelawney whirled around as Roberto let out a snort of laughter.
　　"Let me see that, my dear," she said reprovingly to Diego, sweeping over and snatching Roberto's cup from him. Everyone went quiet to watch.
　　Professor Trelawney was staring into the teacup, rotating it counterclockwise.
　　"The falcon... my dear, you have a deadly enemy."
　　"But everyone knows that, " said Carolina in a loud whisper. Professor Trelawney stared at her.
　　"Well, they do," said Carolina. "Everybody knows about Roberto and You-Know-Who."
　　Roberto and Diego stared at her with a mixture of amazement and admiration. They had never heard Carolina speak to a teacher like that before. Professor Trelawney chose not to reply. She lowered her huge eyes to Roberto's cup again and continued to turn it.
　　"The club... an attack. Dear, dear, this is not a happy cup....
　　I thought that was a bowler hat," said Diego sheepishly.
　　"The skull... danger in your path, my dear...."
　　Everyone was staring, transfixed, at Professor Trelawney, who gave the cup a final turn, gasped, and then screamed.
　　There was another tinkle of breaking china; Mauricio had smashed his second cup. Professor Trelawney sank into a vacant armchair, her glittering hand at her heart and her eyes closed.
　　"My dear boy... my poor, dear boy no it is kinder not to say.. . no... don't ask me...."
　　"What is it, Professor?" said Dean Thomas at once. Everyone had got to their feet, and slowly they crowded around Roberto and Diego's table, pressing close to Professor Trelawney's chair to get a
　　good look at Roberto's cup.
　　"My dear," Professor Trelawney's huge eyes opened dramatically,
　　"You have the Grim."
　　"The what?" said Roberto.
　　He could tell that he wasn't the only one who didn't understand; Dean Thomas shrugged at him and Lavender Brown looked puzzled, but nearly everybody else clapped their hands to their mouths in horror.
　　"The Grim, my dear, the Grim!" cried Professor Trelawney, who looked shocked that Roberto hadn't understood. "The giant, spectral dog that haunts churchyards! My dear boy, it is an omen -- the worst omen -- of death!"
　　Roberto's stomach lurched. That dog on the cover of Death Omens in Flourish and Blotts -the dog in the shadows of Magnolia Crescent... Lavender Brown clapped her hands to her mouth too. Everyone was looking at Roberto, everyone except Carolina, who had gotten up and moved around to the back of Professor Trelawney's chair.
　　"I don't think it looks like a Grim," she said flatly.
　　Professor Trelawney surveyed Carolina with mounting dislike.
　　"You'll forgive me for saying so, my dear, but I perceive very little aura around you. Very little receptivity to the resonances of the future." Seamus Finnigan was tilting his head from side to side.
　　"It looks like a Grim if you do this," he said, with his eyes almost shut, "but it looks more like a donkey from here," he said, leaning to the left.
　　"When you've all finished deciding whether I'm going to die Or not!" said Roberto, taking even himself by surprise. Now nobody seemed to want to look at him.
　　"I think we will leave the lesson here for today," said Professor Trelawney in her mistiest voice. "Yes... please pack away your things...."
　　Silently the class took their teacups back to Professor Trelawney, packed away their books, and closed their bags. Even Diego was avoiding Roberto's eyes.
　　"Until we meet again," said Professor Trelawney faintly, "fair fortune be yours. Oh, and dear" -- she pointed at Mauricio -- "you'll be late next time, so mind you work extra-hard to catch up."
　　Roberto, Diego, and Carolina descended Professor Trelawney's ladder and the winding stair in silence, then set off for Professor Romero's Transfiguration lesson. It took them so long to find her classroom that, early as they had left Divination, they were only just in time.
　　Roberto chose a seat right at the back of the room, feeling as though he were sitting in a very bright spotlight; the rest of the class kept shooting furtive glances at him, as though he were about to drop dead at any moment. He hardly heard what Professor Romero was telling them about Animagi (wizards who could transform at will into animals), and wasn't even watching when she transformed herself in front of their eyes into a tabby cat with spectacle markings around her eyes.
　　"Really, what has got into you all today?" said Professor Romero, turning back into herself with a faint pop, and staring around at them all. "Not that it matters, but that's the first time my transformation's not got applause from a class."
　　Everybody's heads turned toward Roberto again, but nobody spoke. Then Carolina raised her hand.
　　"Please, Professor, we've just had our first Divination class, and we were reading the tea leaves, and --"
　　"Ah, of course," said Professor Romero, suddenly frowning.
　　"There is no need to say any more, Miss Fernandez. Tell me, which of you will be dying this year?"
　　Everyone stared at her.
　　"Me," said Roberto, finally.
　　"I see," said Professor Romero, fixing Roberto with her beady eyes. "Then you should know, Gonzales, that Sibyll Trelawney has predicted the death of one student a year since she arrived at this school. None of them has died yet. Seeing death omens is her favorite way of greeting a new class. If it were not for the fact that I never speak ill of my colleagues --"
　　Professor Romero broke off, and they saw that her nostrils had gone white. She went on, more calmly, "Divination is one of the most imprecise branches of magic. I shall not conceal from you that I have very little patience with it. True Seers are very rare, and Professor Trelawney --"
　　She stopped again, and then said, in a very matter-of-fact tone, "You look in excellent health to me, Gonzales, so you will excuse me if I don't let you off homework today. I assure you that if you die, you need not hand it in."
　　Carolina laughed. Roberto felt a bit better. It was harder to feel scared of a lump of tea leaves away from the dim red light and befuddling perfume of Professor Trelawney's classroom. Not everyone was convinced, however. Diego still looked worried, and Lavender whispered, "But what about Mauricio's cup?"
　　When the Transfiguration class had finished, they joined the crowd thundering toward the Great Hall for lunch.
　　"Diego, cheer up," said Carolina, pushing a dish of stew toward him. "You heard what Professor Romero said."
　　Diego spooned stew onto his plate and picked up his fork but didn't start.
　　"Roberto," he said, in a low, serious voice, "You haven't seen a great black dog anywhere, have you?"
　　"Yeah, I have," said Roberto. "I saw one the night I left the Carrizos'. "
　　Diego let his fork fall with a clatter.
　　"Probably a stray," said Carolina calmly.
　　Diego looked at Carolina as though she had gone mad.
　　"Carolina, if Roberto's seen a Grim, that's -- that's bad," he said. "My -- my uncle Bilius saw one and -- and he died twenty-four hours later!"
　　"Coincidence," said Carolina airily, pouring herself some pumpkin juice.
　　"You don't know what you're talking about!" said Diego, starting to get angry. "Grims scare the living daylights out of most wizards!"
　　"There you are, then," said Carolina in a superior tone. "They see the Grim and die of fright. The Grim's not an omen, it's the cause of death! And Roberto's still with us because he's not stupid enough to see one and think, right, well, I'd better kick the bucket then!"
　　Diego mouthed wordlessly at Carolina, who opened her bag, took out her new Arithmancy book, and propped it open against the juice jug.
　　"I think Divination seems very woolly," she said, searching for her page. "A lot of guesswork, if you ask me."
　　"There was nothing woolly about the Grim in that cup!" said Diego hotly.
　　"You didn't seem quite so confident when you were telling Roberto it was a sheep," said Carolina coolly.
　　"Professor Trelawney said you didn't have the right aura! You just don't like being bad at something for a change!"
　　He had touched a nerve. Carolina slammed her Arithmancy book down on the table so hard that bits of meat and carrot flew everywhere.
　　"If being good at Divination means I have to pretend to see death omens in a lump of tea leaves, I'm not sure I'll be studying it much longer! That lesson was absolute rubbish compared with my Arithmancy class!"
　　She snatched up her bag and stalked away.
　　Diego frowned after her.
　　"What's she talking about?" he said to Roberto. "She hasn't been to an Arithmancy class yet."
　　Roberto was pleased to get out of the castle after lunch. Yesterday's rain had cleared; the sky was a clear, pale gray, and the grass was springy and damp underfoot as they set off for their first ever Care of Magical Creatures class.
　　Diego and Carolina weren't speaking to each other. Roberto walked beside them in silence as they went down the sloping lawns to Villalba's hut on the edge of the Forbidden Forest. It was only when he spotted three only-too- familiar backs ahead of them that he realized they must be having these lessons with the Slytherins. Sosa was talking animatedly to Crabbe and Goyle, who were chortling. Roberto was quite sure he knew what they were talking about.
　　Villalba was waiting for his class at the door of his hut. He stood in his moleskin overcoat, with Fang the boarhound at his heels, looking impatient to start.
　　"C'mon, now, get a move on!" he called as the class approached. "Got a real treat for yeh today! Great lesson comin' up! Everyone here? Right, follow me!"
　　For one nasty moment, Roberto thought that Villalba was going to lead them into the forest; Roberto had had enough unpleasant experiences in there to last him a lifetime. However, Villalba strolled off around the edge of the trees, and five minutes later, they found themselves outside a kind of paddock. There was nothing in there.
　　"Everyone gather 'round the fence here!" he called. "That's it -- make sure yeh can see -- now, firs' thing yeh'll want ter do is open yer books --"
　　"How?" said the cold, drawling voice of Javier Sosa.
　　"Eh?" said Villalba.
　　"How do we open our books?" Sosa repeated. He took out his copy of The Monster Book of Monsters, which he had bound shut with a length of rope. Other people took theirs out too; some, like Roberto, had belted their book shut; others had crammed them inside tight bags or clamped them together with binder clips.
　　"Hasn' -- hasn' anyone bin able ter open their books?" said Villalba, looking crestfallen.
　　The class all shook their heads.
　　"Yeh've got ter stroke 'em," said Villalba, as though this was the most obvious thing in the world. "Look --"
　　He took Carolina's copy and ripped off the Spellotape that bound it. The book tried to bite, but Villalba ran a giant forefinger down its spine, and the book shivered, and then fell open and lay quiet in his hand.
　　"Oh, how silly we've all been!" Sosa sneered. "We should have stroked them! why didn't we guess!"
　　"I -- I thought they were funny," Villalba said uncertainly to Carolina.
　　"Oh, tremendously funny!" said Sosa. "Really witty, giving us books that try and rip our hands off!"
　　"Shut up, Sosa," said Roberto quietly. Villalba was looking downcast and Roberto wanted Villalba's first lesson to be a success.
　　"Righ' then," said Villalba, who seemed to have lost his thread, "so -- so yeh've got yer books an' -- an' - - now yeh need the Magical Creatures. Yeah. So I'll go an' get 'em. Hang on... "
　　He strode away from them into the forest and out of sight.
　　"God, this place is going to the dogs," said Sosa loudly. "That oaf teaching classes, my father'll have a fit when I tell him
　　"Shut up, Sosa," Roberto repeated.
　　"Careful, Gonzales, there's a dementor behind you
　　"Oooooooh!" squealed Lavender Brown, pointing toward the opposite side of the paddock.
　　Trotting toward them were a dozen of the most bizarre creatures Roberto had ever seen. They had the bodies, hind legs, and tails of horses, but the front legs, wings, and heads of what seemed to be giant eagles, with cruel, steel-colored beaks and large, brilliantly, orange eyes. The talons on their front legs were half a foot long and deadly looking. Each of the beasts had a thick leather collar around its neck, which was attached to a long chain, and the ends of all of these were held in the vast hands of Villalba, who came jogging into the paddock behind the creatures.
　　"Gee up, there!" he roared, shaking the chains and urging the creatures toward the fence where the class stood. Everyone drew back slightly as Villalba reached them and tethered the creatures to the fence.
　　"Hippogriffs!" Villalba roared happily, waving a hand at them. "Beau'iful, aren' they?"
　　Roberto could sort of see what Villalba meant. Once you got over the first shock of seeing something that was, half horse, half bird, you started to appreciate the hippogriffs' gleaming coats, changing smoothly from feather to hair, each of them a different color: stormy gray, bronze, pinkish roan, gleaming chestnut, and inky black.
　　"So," said Villalba, rubbing his hands together and beaming around, "if yeh wan' ter come a bit nearer --"
　　No one seemed to want to. Roberto, Diego, and Carolina, however, approached the fence cautiously.
　　"Now, firs' thing yeh gotta know abou' hippogriffs is, they're proud," said Villalba. "Easily offended, hippogriffs are. Don't never insult one, 'cause it might be the last thing yeh do."
　　Sosa, Crabbe, and Goyle weren't listening; they were talking in an undertone and Roberto had a nasty feeling they were plotting how best to disrupt the lesson.
　　"Yeh always wait fer the hippogriff ter make the firs' move," Villalba continued. "It's polite, see? Yeh walk toward him, and yeh bow, an' yeh wait. If he bows back, yeh're allowed ter touch him. If he doesn' bow, then get away from him sharpish, 'cause those talons hurt.
　　"Right -- who wants ter go first?"
　　Most of the class backed farther away in answer. Even Roberto, Diego, and Carolina had misgivings. The hippogriffs were tossing their fierce heads and flexing their powerful wings; they didn't seem to like being tethered like this.
　　"No one?" said Villalba, with a pleading look.
　　"I'll do it," said Roberto.
　　There was an intake of breath from behind him, and both Lavender and Parvati whispered, "Oooh, no, Roberto, remember your tea leaves!"
　　Roberto ignored them. He climbed over the paddock fence.
　　"Good man, Roberto!" roared Villalba. "Right then -- let's see how yeh get on with Buckbeak."
　　He untied one of the chains, pulled the gray hippogriff away from its fellows, and slipped off its leather collar. The class on the other side of the paddock seemed to be holding its breath. Sosa's eyes were narrowed maliciously.
　　"Easy) now, Roberto," said Villalba quietly. "Yeh've got eye contact, now try not ter blink.... Hippogriffs don' trust yeh if yeh blink too much...."
　　Roberto's eyes immediately began to water, but he didn't shut thern. Buckbeak had turned his great, sharp head and was staring at Roberto with one fierce orange eye. "Tha's it," said Villalba. "Tha's it, Roberto... now, bow."
　　Roberto didn't feel much like exposing the back of his neck to Buckbeak, but he did as he was told. He gave a short bow and then looked up.
　　The hippogriff was still staring haughtily at him. It didn't move.
　　"Ah," said Villalba, sounding worried. "Right -- back away, now, Roberto, easy does it
　　But then, to Roberto's enormous surprise, the hippogriff suddenly bent its scaly front knees and sank into what was an unmistakable bow.
　　"Well done, Roberto!" said Villalba, ecstatic. "Right -- yeh can touch him! Pat his beak, go on!"
　　Feeling that a better reward would have been to back away, Roberto moved slowly toward the hippogriff and reached out toward it. He patted the beak several times and the hippogriff closed its eyes lazily, as though enjoying it.
　　The class broke into applause, all except for Sosa, Crabbe, and Goyle, who were looking deeply disappointed.
　　"Righ' then, Roberto," said Villalba. "I reckon he might' let yeh ride him!"
　　This was more than Roberto had bargained for. He was used to a broomstick; but he wasn't sure a hippogriff would be quite the same.
　　"Yeh climb up there, jus' behind the wing joint," said Villalba, "an' mind yeh don' pull any of his feathers out, he won' like that...."
　　Roberto put his foot on the top of Buckbeaks wing and hoisted himself onto its back. Buckbeak stood up. Roberto wasn't sure where to hold on; everything in front of him was covered with feathers.
　　"Go on, then'" roared Villalba, slapping the hippogriffs hindquarters.
　　Without warning, twelve-foot wings flapped open on either side of Roberto, he just had time to seize the hippogriff around the neck before he was soaring upward.
