It was nothing like a broomstick, and Roberto knew which one he preferred; the hippogriff's wings beat uncomfortably on either side of him, catching him under his legs and making him feel he was about to be thrown off; the glossy feathers slipped under his fingers and he didn't dare get a stronger grip; instead of the smooth action of his Nimbus Two Thousand, he now felt himself rocking backward and forward as the hindquarters of the hippogriff rose and fell with its wings.
　　Buckbeak flew him once around the paddock and then headed back to the ground; this was the bit Roberto had been dreading; he leaned back as the smooth neck lowered, feeling he was going to slip off over the beak, then felt a heavy thud as the four ill-assorted feet hit the ground. He just managed to hold on and push himself straight again.
　　"Good work, Roberto!" roared Villalba as everyone except Sosa, Crabbe, and Goyle cheered. "Okay, who else wants a go?"
　　Emboldened by Roberto's success, the rest of the class climbed cautiously into the paddock. Villalba untied the hippogriffs one by one, and soon people were bowing nervously, all over the paddock. Mauricio ran repeatedly backward from his, which didn't seem to want to bend its knees. Diego and Carolina practiced on the chestnut, while Roberto watched.
　　Sosa, Crabbe, and Goyle had taken over Buckbeak. He had bowed to Sosa, who was now patting his beak, looking disdainful.
　　"This is very easy," Sosa drawled, loud enough for Roberto to, hear him. "I knew it must have been, if Gonzales could do it.... I bet you're not dangerous at all, are you?" he said to the hippogriff. "Are you, you great ugly brute?"
　　It happened in a flash of steely talons; Sosa let out a highpitched scream and next moment, Villalba was wrestling Buckbeak back into his collar as he strained to get at Sosa, who lay curled in the grass, blood blossoming over his robes.
　　"I'm dying!" Sosa yelled as the class panicked. "I'm dying, look at me! It's killed me!"
　　"Yer not dyin'!" said Villalba, who had gone very white. "Someone help me -- gotta get him outta here --"
　　Carolina ran to hold open the gate as Villalba lifted Sosa easily. As they passed, Roberto saw that there was a long, deep gash on Sosa's arm; blood splattered the grass and Villalba ran with him, up the slope toward the castle.
　　Very shaken, the Care of Magical Creatures class followed at a walk. The Slytherins were all shouting about Villalba.
　　"They should fire him straight away!" said Pansy Parkinson, who was in tears.
　　"It was Sosa's fault!" snapped Dean Thomas. Crabbe and Goyle flexed their muscles threateningly.
　　They all climbed the stone steps into the deserted entrance hall.
　　"I'm going to see if he's okay!" said Pansy, and they all watched her run up the marble staircase. The Slytherins, still muttering about Villalba, headed away in the direction of their dungeon common room; Roberto, Diego, and Carolina proceeded upstairs to Gryffindor Tower.
　　"You think he'll be all right?" said Carolina nervously.
　　"Course he will. Madam Pomfrey can mend cuts in about a second," said Roberto, who had had far worse injuries mended magically by the nurse.
　　"That was a really bad thing to happen in Villalba's first class, though, wasn't it?" said Diego, looking worried. "Trust Sosa to mess things up for him...."
　　They were among the first to reach the Great Hall at dinnertime, hoping to see Villalba, but he wasn't there.
　　"They wouldn't fire him, would they?" said Carolina anxiously, not touching her steak-and- kidney pudding.
　　"They'd better not," said Diego, who wasn't eating either.
　　Roberto was watching the Slytherin table. A large group including Crabbe and Goyle was huddled together, deep in conversation. Roberto was sure they were cooking up their own version of how Sosa had been injured.
　　"Well, you can't say it wasn't an interesting first day back," said Diego gloomily.
　　They went up to the crowded Gryffindor common room after dinner and tried to do the homework Professor Romero had given them, but all three of them kept breaking off and glancing Out of the tower window.
　　"There's a light on in Villalba's window," Roberto said suddenly.
　　Diego looked at his watch.
　　"If we hurried, we could go down and see him. It's still quite early..."
　　I don't know," Carolina said slowly, and Roberto saw her glance at him.
　　"I'm allowed to walk across the grounds, " he said Pointedly. "Gustavo Martinez hasn't got past the dementors yet, has he?"
　　So they put their things away and headed out of the portrait hole, glad to meet nobody on their way to the front doors, as they weren't entirely sure they were supposed to be out.
　　The grass was still wet and looked almost black in the twilight. When they reached Villalba's hut, they knocked, and a voice growled, "C'min."
　　Villalba was sitting in his shirtsleeves at his scrubbed wooden table; his boarhound, Fang, had his head in Villalba's lap. One look told them that Villalba had been drinking a lot; there was a pewter tankard almost as big as a bucket in front of him, and he seemed to be having difficulty getting them into focus.
　　"'Spect it's a record," he said thickly, when he recognized them. "Don' reckon they've ever had a teacher who lasted on'y a day before."
　　"You haven't been fired, Villalba!" gasped Carolina.
　　"Not yet," said Villalba miserably, taking a huge gulp of whatever was in the tankard. "But's only a matter o' time, i' n't it, after Sosa..."
　　"How is he?" said Diego as they all sat down. "It wasn't serious, was it?"
　　"Madam Pomfrey fixed him best she could," said Villalba dully, "but he's sayin' it's still agony... covered in bandages... moanin'..
　　"He's faking it, " said Roberto at once. "Madam Pomfrey can mend anything. She regrew half my bones last year. Trust Sosa to milk it for all it's worth."
　　"School gov'nors have bin told, o' course," said Villalba miseribly. "They reckon I started too big. Shoulda left hippogriffs fer later... done flobberworms or summat.... Jus' thought itdmake a good firs' lessons all my fault...."
　　"It's all Sosa's fault, Villalba!" said Carolina earnestly.
　　"We're witnesses," said Roberto. "You said hippogriffs attack if you insult them. It's Sosa's problem that he wasn't listening. We'll tell Dominguez what really happened."
　　"Yeah, don't worry, Villalba, we'll back you up," said Diego.
　　Tears leaked out of the crinkled corners of Villalba's beetle-black eyes. He grabbed both Roberto and Diego and pulled them into a bone-breaking hug.
　　"I think you've had enough to drink, Villalba," said Carolina firmly. She took the tankard from the table and went outside to empty it.
　　"At, maybe she's right," said Villalba, letting go of Roberto and Diego, who both staggered away, rubbing their ribs. Villalba heaved himself out of his chair and followed Carolina unsteadily outside. They heard a loud splash.
　　"What's he done?" said Roberto nervously as Carolina came back in with the empty tankard.
　　"Stuck his head in the water barrel," said Carolina, putting the tankard away.
　　Villalba came back, his long hair and beard sopping wet, wiping the water out of his eyes.
　　"That's better," he said, shaking his head like a dog and drenching them all. "Listen, it was good of yeh ter come an' see me, I really --
　　Villalba stopped dead, staring at Roberto as though he'd only just realized he was there.
　　"WHAT D'YEH THINK YOU'RE DOIN', EH?" he roared, so suddenly that they jumped a foot in the air. "YEH'RE NOT TO GO WANDERIN' AROUND AFTER DARK, ROBERTO! AN, YOU TWO! LETTIN' HIM!"
　　Villalba strode over to Roberto, grabbed his arm, and pulled him to the door.
　　"C'mon!" Villalba said angrily. "I'm takin' yer all back up ter school, an' don' let me catch yeh walkin' down ter see me after dark again. I'm not worth that!"
　　CHAPTER SEVEN
　　THE BOGGART IN THE WARDROBE
　　Sosa didn't reappear in classes until late on Thursday morning, when the Slytherins and Gryffindors were halfway through double Potions. He swaggered into the dungeon, his right arm covered in bandages and bound up in a sling, acting, in Roberto's opinion, as though he were the heroic survivor of some dreadful battle.
　　"How is it, Javier?" simpered Pansy Parkinson. "Does it hurt much?"
　　"Yeah," said Sosa, putting on a brave sort of grimace. But Roberto saw him wink at Crabbe and Goyle when Pansy had looked away.
　　"Settle down, settle down," said Professor Peralta idly.
　　Roberto and Diego scowled at each other; Peralta wouldn't have said "settle down" if they'd walked in late, he'd have given them detention. But Sosa had always been able to get away with anything in Peralta's classes; Peralta was head of Slytherin House, and generality favored his own students above all others.
　　They were making a new potion today, a Shrinking Solution. Sosa set up his cauldron right next to Roberto and Diego, so that they were preparing their ingredients on the same table.
　　"Sir," Sosa called, "sir, I'll need help cutting up these daisy roots, because of my arm --"
　　"Lopez, cut up Sosa's roots for him," said Peralta without looking up.
　　Diego went brick red.
　　"There's nothing wrong with your arm," he hissed at Sosa.
　　Sosa smirked across the table.
　　"Lopez, you heard Professor Peralta; cut up these roots."
　　Diego seized his knife, pulled Sosa's roots toward him, and began to chop them roughly, so that they were all different sizes.
　　"Professor," drawled Sosa, "Lopez's mutilating my roots, sit."
　　Peralta approached their table, stared down his hooked nose at the roots, then gave Diego an unpleasant smile from beneath his long, greasy black hair.
　　"Mejiae roots with Sosa, Lopez."
　　"But, sit --!"
　　Diego had spent the last quarter of an hour carefully shredding his own roots into exactly equal pieces.
　　"Now," said Peralta in his most dangerous voice.
　　Diego shoved his own beautifully cut roots across the table a, Sosa, then took up the knife again.
　　"And, sir, I'll need this shrivelfig skinned," said Sosa, his voice full of malicious laughter.
　　"Gonzales, you can skin Sosa's shrivelfig," said Peralta, giving Roberto the look of loathing he always reserved just for him.
　　Roberto took Sosa's shrivelfig as Diego began trying to repair the damage to the roots he now had to use. Roberto skinned the shrivelfig as fast as he could and flung it back across the table at Sosa without speaking. Sosa was smirking more broadly than ever.
　　"Seen your pal Villalba lately?" he asked them quietly.
　　"None of your business," said Diego jerkily, without looking up.
　　"I'm afraid he won't be a teacher much longer," said Sosa in a tone of mock sorrow. "Father's not very happy about my injury --"
　　"Keep talking, Sosa, and I'll give you a real injury," snarled Diego.
　　"- he's complained to the school governors. And to the Ministry of Magic. Father's got a lot of influence, you know. And a lasting injury like this" -- he gave a huge, fake sigh -- "who knows if my arm'll ever be the same again?"
　　"So that's why you're putting it on," said Roberto, accidentally beheading a dead caterpillar because his hand was shaking in anger. "To try to get Villalba fired."
　　"Well," said Sosa, lowering his voice to a whisper, "partly, Gonzales. But there are other benefits too. Lopez, slice my caterpillars for me."
　　A few cauldrons away, Mauricio was in trouble. Mauricio regularly went to pieces in Potions lessons; it was his worst subject, and his great fear of Professor Peralta made things ten times worse. His potion, which was supposed to be a bright, acid green, had turned --
　　"Orange, Silva," said Peralta, ladling some up and allowing to splash back into the cauldron, so that everyone could see.
　　"Orange. Tell me, boy, does anything penetrate that thick skull of yours? Didn't you hear me say, quite clearly, that only one -tat spleen was needed? Didn't I state plainly that a dash of leech juice would suffice? What do I have to do to make you understand, Silva?"
　　Mauricio was pink and trembling. He looked as though he was on the verge of tears.
　　"Please, sir," said Carolina, "please, I could help Mauricio put it right --"
　　"I don't remember asking you to show off, Miss Fernandez," said Peralta coldly, and Carolina went as pink as Mauricio. "Silva, at the end of this lesson we will feed a few drops of this potion to your toad and see what happens. Perhaps that will encourage you to do it properly."
　　Peralta moved away, leaving Mauricio breathless with fear.
　　"Help me!" he moaned to Carolina.
　　"Hey, Roberto," said Seamus Finnigan, leaning over to borrow Roberto's brass scales, "have you heard? Daily Prophet this morning -- they reckon Gustavo Martinez's been sighted."
　　"Where?" said Roberto and Diego quickly. On the other side of the table, Sosa looked up, listening closely.
　　"Not too far from here," said Seamus, who looked excited. "It was a Muggle who saw him. 'Course, she didn't really understand. The Muggles think he's just an ordinary criminal, don't they? So she phoned the telephone hot line. By the time the Ministry of Magic got there, he was gone."
　　"Not too far from here... " Diego repeated, looking significantly at Roberto. He turned around and saw Sosa watching closely. "What, Sosa? Need something else skinned?"
　　But Sosa's eyes were shining malevolently, and they were fixed Roberto. He leaned across the table.
　　Martinez single-handed, Gonzales?"
　　"Thinking Of trying to catch
　　"Yeah, that's right," said Roberto offhandedly.
　　Sosas thin mouth was curving in a mean smile.
　　"Of course, if it was me," he said quietly, "I'd have done something before now. I wouldn't be staying in school like a good boy, I'd be out there looking for him."
　　"What are you talking about, Sosa?" said Diego roughly.
　　"Don't you know, Gonzales?" breathed Sosa, his pate eyes narrowed.
　　"Know what?"
　　Sosa let out a low, sneering laugh.
　　"Maybe you'd rather not risk your neck," he said. "Want to leave it to the dementors, do you? But if it was me, I'd want revenge. I'd hunt him down myself."
　　"What are you talking about?" said Roberto angrily, but at that moment Peralta called, "You should have finished adding your ingredients by now; this potion needs to stew before it can be drunk, so clear away while it simmers and then we'll test Silva's... "
　　Crabbe and Goyle laughed openly, watching Mauricio sweat as he stirred his potion feverishly. Carolina was muttering instructions to him out of the corner of her mouth, so that Peralta wouldn't see. Roberto and Diego packed away their unused ingredients and went to wash their hands and ladles in the stone basin in the corner.
　　"What did Sosa mean?" Roberto muttered to Diego as he stuck his hands under the icy jet that poured from the gargoyle's mouth "Why would I want revenge on Martinez? He hasn't done anything to me -- yet.
　　"He's making it up," said Diego savagely. "He's trying to make you do something stupid...."
　　The end of the lesson in sight, Peralta strode over to Mauricio, who was cowering by his cauldron.
　　"Everyone gather 'round," said Peralta, his black eyes glittering, and watch what happens to Silva's toad. If he has managed to produce a Shrinking Solution, it will shrink to a tadpole. If, as I don't doubt, he has done it wrong, his toad is likely to be poisoned."
　　The Gryffindors watched fearfully. The Slytherins looked excited. Peralta picked up Trevor the toad in his left hand and dipped a small spoon into Mauricio's potion, which was now green. He trickled a few drops down Trevor's throat.
　　There was a moment of hushed silence, in which Trevor gulped; then there was a small pop, and Trevor the tadpole was wriggling in Peralta's palm.
　　The Gryffindors burst into applause. Peralta, looking sour, pulled a small bottle from the pocket of his robe, poured a few drops on top of Trevor, and he reappeared suddenly, fully grown.
　　"Five points from Gryffindor," said Peralta, which wiped the smiles from every face. "I told you not to help him, Miss Fernandez. Class dismissed."
　　Roberto, Diego, and Carolina climbed the steps to the entrance hall. Roberto was still thinking about what Sosa had said, while Diego was seething about Peralta.
　　"Five points from Gryffindor because the potion was all right!
　　Why didn't You lie, Carolina? You should've said Mauricio did it all by himself!"
　　Carolina didn't answer. Diego looked around.
　　"Where is she?"
　　Roberto turned too. They were at the top of the steps now, watching the rest of the class pass them, heading for the Great Hall and lunch.
　　"She was right behind us," said Diego, frowning.
　　Sosa passed them, walking between Crabbe and Goyle. He smirked at Roberto and disappeared.
　　"There she is," said Roberto.
　　Carolina was panting slightly, hurrying up the stairs; one hand clutched her bag, the other seemed to be tucking something down the front of her robes.
　　"How did you do that?" said Diego.
　　"What?" said Carolina, joining them.
　　"One minute you were right behind us, the next moment, you were back at the bottom of the stairs again."
　　"What?" Carolina looked slightly confused. "Oh -- I had to go back for something. Oh no --"
　　A seam had split on Carolina's bag. Roberto wasn't surprised; he could see that it was crammed with at least a dozen large and heavy books.
　　"Why are you carrying all these around with you?" Diego asked her.
　　"You know how many subjects I'm taking," said Carolina breathlessly. "Couldn't hold these for me, could you?"
　　"But --" Diego was turning over the books she had handed him, looking at the covers. "You havent got any of these subjects today. It's only Defense Against the Dark Arts this afternoon."
　　"Oh yes," said Carolina vaguely, but she packed all the books back into her bag just the same. I hope there's something good for lunch, I'm starving," she added, and she marched off toward the Great Hall.
　　"D'you get the feeling Carolina's not telling us something?Diego asked Roberto.
　　Professor Cardozo wasn't there when they arrived at his first Defense Against the Dark Arts lesson. They all sat down, took out their books, quills, and parchment, and were talking when he finally entered the room. Cardozo smiled vaguely and placed his tatty old briefcase on the teacher's desk. He was as shabby as ever but looked healthier than he had on the train, as though he had had a few square meals.
　　"Good afternoon," he said. "Would you please put all your books back in your bags. Today's will be a practical lesson. You will need only your wands."
　　A few curious looks were exchanged as the class put away their books. They had never had a practical Defense Against the Dark Arts before, unless you counted the memorable class last year when their old teacher had brought a cageful of pixies -to class and set them loose.
　　"Right then," said Professor Cardozo, when everyone was ready. "If you'd follow me."
　　Puzzled but interested, the class got to its feet and followed Professor Cardozo out of the classroom. He led them along the deserted corridor and around a corner, where the first thing they saw was Peeves the Poltergeist, who was floating upside down in midair and stuffing the nearest keyhole with chewing gum.
　　Peeves didn't look up until Professor Cardozo was two feet away; ,hen he wiggled his curly-toed feet and broke into song.
　　"Loony, loopy Cardozo," Peeves sang. "Loony, loopy Cardozo, loony, loopy Cardozo --"
　　Rude and unmanageable as he almost always was, Peeves usually showed some respect toward the teachers. Everyone looked quickly at Professor Cardozo to see how he would take this; to their surprise, he was still smiling.
　　"I'd take that gum out of the keyhole if I were you, Peeves," he said pleasantly. "Mr. Filch won't be able to get in to his brooms."
　　Filch was the Hogwarts caretaker, a bad-tempered, failed wizard who waged a constant war against the students and, indeed, Peeves. However, Peeves paid no attention to Professor Cardozo's words, except to blow a loud wet raspberry.
　　Professor Cardozo gave a small sigh and took out his wand.
　　"This is a useful little spell, he told the class over his shoulder. "Please watch closely."
　　He raised the wand to shoulder height, said, "Waddiwasi! "and pointed it at Peeves.
　　With the force of a bullet, the wad of chewing gum shot out of the keyhole and straight down Peeves's left nostril; he whirled upright and zoomed away, cursing.
　　"Cool, sit!" said Dean Thomas in amazement.
　　"Thank you, Dean," said Professor Cardozo, putting his wand away again. "Shall we proceed?"
　　They set off again, the class looking at shabby Professor Cardozo with increased respect. He led them down a second corridor and stopped, right outside the staffroom door.
　　"Inside, please," said Professor Cardozo, opening it and standing back.
　　The staffroom, a long, paneled room full of old, mismatched chairs, was empty except for one teacher. Professor Peralta was sitting in a low armchair, and he looked around as the class filed in. His eyes were glittering and there was a nasty sneer playing around his mouth. As Professor Cardozo came in and made to close the door behind him, Peralta said, "Leave it open, Cardozo. I'd rather not witness this."
　　He got to his feet and strode past the class, his black robes billowing behind him. At the doorway he turned on his heel and said, "Possibly no one's warned you, Cardozo, but this class contains Mauricio Silva. I would advise you not to entrust him with anything difficult. Not unless Miss Fernandez is hissing instructions in his ear."
　　Mauricio went scarlet. Roberto glared at Peralta; it was bad enough that he bullied Mauricio in his own classes, let alone doing it in front of other teachers.
　　Professor Cardozo had raised his eyebrows.
　　"I was hoping that Mauricio would assist me with the first stage of the operation," he said, "and I am sure he will perform it admirably."
　　Mauricio's face went, if possible, even redder. Peralta's lip curled, but he left, shutting the door with a snap.
　　"Now, then," said Professor Cardozo, beckoning the class toward the end of the room, where there was nothing but an old wardrobe where the teachers kept their spare robes. As Professor Cardozo went to stand next to it, the wardrobe gave a sudden wobble, banging off the wall.
　　"Nothing to worry about," said Professor Cardozo calmly because a few people had jumped backward in alarm. "There's a boggart in there."
　　Most people seemed to feel that this was something to worry about. Mauricio gave Professor Cardozo a look of pure terror, and Seamus Finnigan eyed the now rattling doorknob apprehensively.
　　"Boggarts like dark, enclosed spaces," said Professor Cardozo. "Wardrobes, the gap beneath beds, the cupboards under sinks -- I've even met one that had lodged itself in a grandfather clock. This one moved in yesterday afternoon, and I asked the headmaster if the staff would leave it to give my third years some practice.
　　"So, the first question we must ask ourselves is, what is a boggart?"
　　Carolina put up her hand.
　　"It's a shape-shifter," she said. "It can take the shape of whatever it thinks will frighten us most."
　　"Couldn't have put it better myself," said Professor Cardozo, and Carolina glowed. "So the boggart sitting in the darkness within has not yet assumed a form. He does not yet know what will frighten the person on the other side of the door. Nobody knows what a boggart looks like when he is alone, but when I let him out, he will immediately become whatever each of us most fears.
　　"This means," said Professor Cardozo, choosing to ignore Mauricio's 'mall sputter of terror, "that we have a huge advantage over the boggart before we begin. Have you spotted it, Roberto?"
　　Trying to answer a question with Carolina next to him, bobbing up and down on the balls of her feet with her hand in the air, was very off-putting, but Roberto had a go.
　　"Er -- because there are so many of us, it won't know what shape it should be?"
　　"Precisely," said Professor Cardozo, and Carolina put her hand down, looking a little disappointed. "It's always best to have com pany when you're dealing with a boggart. He becomes confused. Which should he become, a headless corpse or a flesh-eating slug? I once saw a boggart make that very mistake -- tried to frighten two people at once and turned himself into half a slug. Not remotely frightening.
　　"The charm that repels a boggart is simple, yet it requires force of mind. You see, the thing that really finishes a boggart is laughter. What you need to do is force it to assume a shape that you find amusing.
　　"We will practice the charm without wands first. After me, please ... Riddikulus!"
　　"Riddikulus!" said the class together.
　　"Good," said Professor Cardozo. "Very good. But that was the easy part, I'm afraid. You see, the word alone is not enough. And this is where you come in, Mauricio."
　　The wardrobe shook again, though not as much as Mauricio, who walked forward as though he were heading for the gallows.
　　"Right, Mauricio," said Professor Cardozo. "First things first: what would you say is the thing that frightens you most in the world?"
　　Mauricio's lips moved, but no noise came out.
　　"didn't catch that, Mauricio, sorry," said Professor Cardozo cheerfully.
　　Mauricio looked around rather wildly, as though begging someone to help him, then said, in barely more than a whisper, "Professor Peralta."
　　Nearly everyone laughed. Even Mauricio grinned apologetically. Professor Cardozo, however, looked thoughtful.
　　"Professor Peralta... hmmm... Mauricio, I believe you live with your grandmother?"
　　"Er -- yes," said Mauricio nervously. "But -- I don't want the boggart to turn into her either."
　　"No, no, you misunderstand me," said Professor Cardozo, now smiling. "I wonder, could you tell us what sort of clothes your grandmother usually wears?"
　　Mauricio looked startled, but said, "Well... always the same hat. A tall one with a stuffed vulture on top. And a long dress... green, normally... and sometimes a fox-fur scarf."
　　"And a handbag?" prompted Professor Cardozo.
　　"A big red one," said Mauricio.
　　"Right then," said Professor Cardozo. "Can you picture those clothes very clearly, Mauricio? Can you see them in your mind's eye?"
　　"Yes," said Mauricio uncertainty, plainly wondering what was coming next.
　　"When the boggart bursts out of this wardrobe, Mauricio, and sees You, it will assume the form of Professor Peralta," said Cardozo. "And You will raise your wand -- thus -- and cry 'Riddikulus' -- and concentrate hard on your grandmother's clothes. If all goes well, Professor Boggart Peralta will be forced into that vulture-topped hat, and that green dress, with that big red handbag."
　　There was a great shout of laughter. The wardrobe wobbled more violently.
　　"If Mauricio is successful, the boggart is likely to shift his attention to each of us in turn," said Professor Cardozo. "I would like all of you to take a moment now to think of the thing that scares you most, and imagine how you might force it to look comical...."
　　The room went quiet. Roberto thought... 'What scared him most in the world?
　　His first thought was Lord Salvador -- a Salvador returned to full strength. But before he had even started to plan a possible counterattack on a boggart-Salvador, a horrible image came floating to the surface of his mind....
　　A rotting, glistening hand, slithering back beneath a black cloak ... a long, rattling breath from an unseen mouth... then a cold so penetrating it felt like drowning....
　　Roberto shivered, then looked around, hoping no one had noticed. Many people had their eyes shut tight. Diego was muttering to himself, "Take its legs off " Roberto was sure he knew what that was about. Diego's greatest fear was spiders.
　　"Everyone ready?" said Professor Cardozo.
　　Roberto felt a lurch of fear. He wasn't ready. How could you make a dementor less frightening? But he didn't want to ask for more time; everyone else was nodding and rolling up their sleeves.
　　"Mauricio, we're going to back away," said Professor Cardozo. "Let you have a clear field, all right? I'll call the next person forward.... Everyone back, now, so Mauricio can get a clear shot --"
　　They all retreated, backed against the walls, leaving Mauricio alone beside the wardrobe. He looked pale and frightened, but he had pushed up the sleeves of his robes and was holding his wand ready.
　　"On the count of three, Mauricio," said Professor Cardozo, who was
　　pointing his own wand at the handle of the wardrobe. "One two -- three -- now!"
　　A jet of sparks shot from the end of Professor Cardozo's wand and hit the doorknob. The wardrobe burst open. Hook-nosed and menacing, Professor Peralta stepped out, his eyes flashing at Mauricio.
　　Mauricio backed away, his wand up, mouthing wordlessly. Peralta was bearing down upon him, reaching inside his robes.
　　"R -- r -- riddikulus! "squeaked Mauricio.
　　There was a noise like a whip crack. Peralta stumbled; he was wearing a long, lace-trimmed dress and a towering hat topped with a moth-eaten vulture, and he was swinging a huge crimson handbag.
　　There was a roar of laughter; the boggart paused, confused, and Professor Cardozo shouted, "Parvati! Forward!"
　　Parvati walked forward, her face set. Peralta rounded on her. There was another crack, and where he had stood was a bloodstained, bandaged mummy; its sightless face was turned to Parvati and it began to walk toward her very slowly, dragging its feet, its stiff arms rising --
　　"Riddikulus!" cried Parvati.
　　A bandage unraveled at the mummy's feet; it became entangled, fell face forward, and its head rolled off.
　　"Seamus!" roared Professor Cardozo.
　　Seamus darted past Parvati.
　　Crack! Where the mummy had been was a woman with floorlength black hair and a skeletal, green-tinged face -- a banshee. She opened her mouth wide and an unearthly sound filled the room, a long, wailing shriek that made the hair on Roberto's head stand on end -- 'Riddikulus!" shouted Seamus.
　　The banshee made a rasping noise and clutched her throat; her voice was gone.
　　Crack! The banshee turned into a rat, which chased its tail in a circle, then -- crack!- became a rattlesnake, which slithered and writhed before -- crack! -- becoming a single, bloody eyeball.
　　'It's confused!" shouted Cardozo. "We're getting there! Dean!"
　　Dean hurried forward.
　　Crack! The eyeball became a severed hand, which flipped over and began to creep along the floor like a crab.
　　"Riddikulus!" yelled Dean.
　　'There was a snap, and the hand was trapped in a mousetrap.
　　"Excellent! Diego, you next!"
　　Diego leapt forward.
　　Crack!
　　Quite a few people screamed. A giant spider, six feet tall and covered in hair, was advancing on Diego, clicking its pincers menacingly. For a moment, Roberto thought Diego had frozen. Then --
　　"Riddikulus!" bellowed Diego, and the spider's legs vanished; it rolled over and over; Lavender Brown squealed and ran out of its way and it came to a halt at Roberto's feet. He raised his wand, ready, but --
　　"Here!" shouted Professor Cardozo suddenly, hurrying forward. Crack!
　　The legless spider had vanished. For a second, everyone looked wildly around to see where it was. Then they saw a silvery-white orb hanging in the air in front of Cardozo, who said, "Riddikulus!" almosi lazily.
　　Crack!
　　"Forward, Mauricio, and finish him off!" said Cardozo as the boggart landed on the floor as a cockroach. Crack! Peralta was back. This time Mauricio charged forward looking determined.
　　"Riddikulus!" he shouted, and they had a split second's view of Peralta in his lacy dress before Mauricio let out a great "Ha!" of laughter, and the boggart exploded, burst into a thousand tiny wisps of smoke, and was gone.
　　"Excellent!" cried Professor Cardozo as the class broke into applause. "Excellent) Mauricio. Well done, everyone.... Let me See... five points to Gryffindor for every person to tackle the boggart -- ten for Mauricio because he did it twice... and five each to Carolina and Roberto."
　　"But I didn't do anything," said Roberto.
　　"You and Carolina answered my questions correctly at the start of the class, Roberto," Cardozo said lightly. "Very well, everyone, an excellent lesson. Homework, kindly read the chapter on boggarts and summarize it for me... to be handed in on Monday. That will be all."
　　Talking excitedly, the class left the staffroom. Roberto, however, wasn't feeling cheerful. Professor Cardozo had deliberately stopped him from tackling the boggart. Why? Was it because he'd seen Roberto collapse on the train, and thought he wasn't up to much? Had he thought Roberto would pass out again?
　　But no one else seemed to have noticed anything.
　　"Did you see me take that banshee?" shouted Seamus. "And the hand!" said Dean, waving his own around.
　　"And Peralta in that hat!" "And my mummy!"
　　I wonder why Professor Cardozo's frightened of crystal balls?" said Lavender thoughtfully.
　　"That was the best Defense Against the Dark Arts lesson we've ever had, wasn't it?" said Diego excitedly as they made their way back to the classroom to get their bags.
　　"He seems like a very good teacher," said Carolina approvingly. "But I wish I could have had a turn with the boggart --"
　　"What would it have been for you?" said Diego, sniggering. "A piece of homework that only got nine out of ten?"
　　CHAPTER EIGHT
　　FLIGHT OF THE FAT FADY
　　In no time at all, Defense Against the Dark Arts had become most people's favorite class. Only Javier Sosa and his gang of Slytherins had anything bad to say about Professor Cardozo.
　　"Look at the state of his robes," Sosa would say in a loud whisper as Professor Cardozo passed. "He dresses like our old houseelf "
　　But no one else cared that Professor Cardozo's robes were patched and frayed. His next few lessons were just as interesting as the first. After boggarts, they studied Red Caps, nasty little goblin like creatures that lurked wherever there had been bloodshed: in the dungeons of castles and the potholes of deserted battlefields, waiting to bludgeon those who had gotten lost. From Red Caps they moved on to kappas, creepy. water-dwellers that looked like scaly monkeys, with webbed hands itching to strangle unwitting waders in their ponds.
　　Roberto only wished he was as happy with some of his other classes. Worst of all was Potions. Peralta was in a particularly vindictive mood these days, and no one was in any doubt why. The story of the boggart assuming Peralta's shape, and the way that Mauricio had dressed it in his grandmother's clothes, had traveled through the school like wildfire. Peralta didn't seem to find it funny. His eyes flashed menacingly at the very mention of Professor Cardozo's name, and he was bullying Mauricio worse than ever.
　　Roberto was also growing to dread the hours he spent in Professor Trelawney's stifling tower room, deciphering lopsided shapes and symbols, trying to ignore the way Professor Trelawney's enormous eyes filled with tears every time she looked at him. He couldn't like Professer Trelawney, even though she was treated with respect bordering on reverence by many of the class. Parvati Patil and Lavender Brown had taken to haunting Professor Trelawney's tower room at lunch times, and always returned with annoyingly superior looks on their faces, as though they knew things the others didn't. They had also started using hushed voices whenever they spoke to Roberto, as though he were on his deathbed.
　　Nobody really liked Care of Magical Creatures, which, after the action-packed first class, had become extremely dull. Villalba seemed to have lost his confidence. They were now spending lesson after lesson learning how to look after flobberworms, which had to be some of the most boring creatures in existence.
　　"Why would anyone bother looking after them?" said Diego, after yet another hour of poking shredded lettuce down the flobberworms' throats.
　　At the start of October, however, Roberto had something else to occupy him, something so enjoyable it more than made up for his unsatisfactory classes. The Quidditch season was approaching, and O1iver Wood, Captain of the Gryffindor team, called a meeting on Thursday evening to discuss tactics for the new season.
　　There were seven people on a Quidditch team: three Chasers, whose job it was to score goals by putting the Quaffle (a red, soccer-sized ball) through one of the fifty-foot-high hoops at each
　　end of the field; two Beaters, who were equipped with heavy bats to repel the Bludgers (two heavy black balls that zoomed around trying to attack the players); a Keeper, who defended the goal
　　posts, and the Seeker, who had the hardest job of all, that of catching the Golden Snitch, a tiny, winged, walnut-sized ball, whose capture ended the game and earned the Seeker's team an extra one hundred and fifty points.
　　Oliver Wood was a burly seventeen-year-old, now in his seventh and final year at Hogwarts. There was a quiet sort of desperation in his voice a's he addressed his six fellow team members in the chilly locker rooms on the edge of the darkening Quidditch field.
　　"This is our last chance -- my last chance -- to win the Quidditch Cup," he told them, striding up and down in front of them. "I'll be leaving at the end of this year. I'll never get another shot at it."
　　"Gryffindor hasn't won for seven years now. Okay, so we've had the worst luck in the world -- injuries -- then the tournamentgetting called off last year Wood swallowed, as though the memory still brought a lump to his throat. "But we also know we've got the best-ruddy-team-in-the-school," he said, punching a fist into his other hand, the old manic glint back in his eye. "We've got three superb Chasers."
　　Wood pointed at Alicia Spinner, Angelina Johnson, and Katie Bell.
　　"We've got two unbeatable Beaters."
　　"Stop it, Oliver, you're embarrassing us," said Pablo and Luis Lopez together, pretending to blush.
　　"And we've got a Seeker who has never failed to win us a match!" Wood rumbled, glaring at Roberto with a kind of furious pride. "And me," he added as an afterthought.
　　"We think you're very good too, Oliver," said Luis.
　　"Spanking good Keeper," said Pablo.
　　"The point is," Wood went on, resuming his pacing, "the Quidditch Cup should have had our name on it these last two years. Ever since Roberto joined the team, I've thought the thing was in the bag. But we haven't got it, and this year's the last chance we'll get to finally see our name on the thing...."
　　Wood spoke so dejectedly that even Pablo and Luis looked sympathetic.
　　"Oliver, this year's our year," said Pablo.
　　"We'll do it, Oliver!" said Angelina.
　　"Definitely," said Roberto.
　　Full of determination, the team started training sessions, three evenings a week. The weather was getting colder and wetter, the nights darker, but no amount of mud, wind, or rain could tarnish Roberto's wonderful vision of finally winning the huge, silver Quidditch Cup.
　　Roberto returned to the Gryffindor common room one evening after training, cold and stiff but pleased with the way practice had gone, to find the room buzzing excitedly.
　　"What's happened?", he asked Diego and Carolina, who were sitting in two of the best chairs by the fireside and completing some star charts for Astronomy.
　　"First Hogsmeade weekend," said Diego, pointing at a notice that had appeared on the battered old bulletin board. "End of October. Halloween."
　　"Excellent," said Pablo, who had followed Roberto through the portrait hole. "I need to visit Zonko's. I'm nearly out of Stink Pellets."
　　Roberto threw himself into a chair beside Diego, his high spirits ebbing away. Carolina seemed to read his mind.
　　"Roberto, I'm sure you'll be able to go next time," she said. "They're bound to catch Martinez soon. He's been sighted once already."
　　"Martinez's not fool enough to try anything in Hogsmeade," said Diego. "Ask Romero if you can go this time, Roberto. The next one might not be for ages --"
　　"Diego!" said Carolina. "Roberto's supposed to stay in school-"
　　"He can't be the only third year left behind," said Diego. "Ask Romero, go on, Roberto --"
　　"Yeah, I think I will," said Roberto, making up his mind.
　　Carolina opened her mouth to argue, but at that moment Crookshanks leapt lightly onto her lap. A large, dead spider was dangling from his mouth.
　　"Does he have to eat that in front of us?" said Diego, scowling.
　　"Clever Crookshanks, did you catch that all by yourself?" said Carolina.
　　Crookshanks; slowly chewed up the spider, his yellow eyes fixed insolently on Diego.
　　"Just keep him over there, that's all," said Diego irritably, turning back to his star chart. "1've got Scabbers asleep in my bag."
　　Roberto yawned. He really wanted to go to bed, but he still had his own star chart to complete. He pulled his bag toward him, took out parchment, ink, and quill, and started work.
　　"You can copy mine, if you like," said Diego, labeling his last star with a flourish and shoving the chart toward Roberto.
　　Carolina, who disapproved of copying, pursed her lips but didn't say anything. Crookshanks was still staring unblinkingly at Diego, flicking the end of his bushy tail. Then, without warning, he pounced.
　　"OY!" Diego roared, seizing his bag as Crookshanks sank four sets of claws deep inside it and began tearing ferociously. "GET OFF, YOU STUPID ANIMAL!"
　　Diego tried to pull the bag away from Crookshanks, but Crookshanks clung on, spitting and slashing.
　　"Diego, don't hurt him!" squealed Carolina; the whole common room was watching; Diego whirled the bag around, Crookshanks still clinging to it, and Scabbers came flying out of the top -
　　"CATCH THAT CAR' Diego yelled as Crookshanks freed himself from the remnants of the bag, sprang over the table, and chased after the terrified Scabbers.
　　Luis Lopez made a lunge for Crookshanks but missed; Scabbers streaked through twenty pairs of legs and shot beneath an old chest of drawers. Crookshanks skidded to a halt, crouched low on his bandy legs, and started making furious swipes beneath it with his front paw.
　　Diego and Carolina hurried over; Carolina grabbed Crookshanks around the middle and heaved him away; Diego threw himself onto his stomach and, with great difficulty, pulled Scabbers out by the tail.
　　"Look at him!" he said furiously to Carolina, dangling Scabbers in front of her. "He's skin and bone! You keep that cat away from him!"
　　"Crookshanks doesn't understand it's wrong!" said Carolina, her voice shaking. "All cats chase rats, Diego!"
　　"There's something funny about that animal!" said Diego, who was trying to persuade a frantically wiggling Scabbers back into his pocket. "It heard me say that Scabbers was in my bag!"
　　"Oh, what rubbish," said Carolina impatiently. "Crookshanks could smell him, Diego, how else d'you think --"
　　"That cat's got it in for Scabbers!" said Diego, 'ignoring the people around him, who were starting to giggle. "And Scabbers was here first, and he's ill!"
　　Diego marched through the common room and out of sight up the stairs to the boys' dormitories.
　　Diego was still in a bad mood with Carolina next day. He barely talked to her all through Herbology, even though he, Roberto, and Carolina were working together on the same puffapod.
　　"How's Scabbers?" Carolina asked timidly as they stripped fat pink pods from the plants and emptied the shining beans into a wooden pail.
　　"He's hiding at the bottom of my bed, shaking, " said Diego angrily, missing the pail and scattering beans over the greenhouse floor.
　　"Careful, Lopez, careful!" cried Professor Sprout as the beans burst into bloom before their very eyes.
　　They had Transfiguration next. Roberto, who had resolved to ask Professor Romero after the lesson whether he could go into Hogsmeade with the rest, joined the line outside the class trying to decide how he was going to argue his case. He was distracted, however, by a disturbance at the front of the line.
　　Lavender Brown seemed to be crying. Parvati had her arm around her and was explaining something to Seamus Finnigan and Dean Thomas, who were looking very serious.
　　"What's the matter, Lavender?" said Carolina anxiously as she, Roberto, and Diego went to join the group.
　　"She got a letter from home this morning," Parvati whispered. "It's her rabbit, Binky. He's been killed by a fox."
　　"Oh," said Carolina, "I'm sorry, Lavender."
　　"I should have known!" said Lavender tragically. "You know what day it is?"
　　"Er --"
　　"The sixteenth of October! 'That thing you're dreading, it will happen on the sixteenth of October!' Remember? She was right, she was right!"
　　The whole class was gathered around Lavender now. Seamus shook his head seriously. Carolina hesitated; then she said, "You -- you were dreading Binky being killed by a fox?"
　　"Well, not necessarily by a fox," said Lavender, looking up at Carolina with streaming eyes, "but I was obviously dreading him dying, wasn't l?"
　　"Oh," said Carolina. She paused again. Then
　　"Was Binky an old rabbit?"
　　"N -- no!" sobbed Lavender. "H -- he was only a baby!"
　　Parvati tightened her arm around Lavender's shoulders.
　　"But then, why would you dread him dying?" said Carolina.
　　Parvati glared at her.
　　"Well, look at it logically," said Carolina, turning to the rest of the group- "I mean, Binky didn't even die today, did he? Lavender just got the news today-" Lavender wailed loudly. "- and she can't have been dreading it, because it's come as a real shock --"
　　"Don't mind Carolina, Lavender," said Diego loudly, "she doesn't think other people's pets matter very much."
　　Professor Romero opened the classroom door at that moment, which was perhaps lucky; Carolina and Diego were looking daggers at each other, and when they got into class, they seated themselves on either side of Roberto and didn't talk to each other for the whole class.
　　Roberto still hadn't decided what he was going to say to Professor Romero when the bell rang at the end of the lesson, but it was she who brought up the subject of Hogsmeade first.
　　"One moment, please !" she called as the class made to leave. "As you're all in my House, you should hand Hogsmeade permission forms to me before Halloween. No form, no visiting the village, so don't forget!"
　　Mauricio put up his hand.
　　"Please, Professor, I -- I think I've lost
　　"Your grandmother sent yours to me directly, Silva," said Professor Romero. "She seemed to think it was safer. Well, that's all, you may leave."
　　"Ask her now," Diego hissed at Roberto.
　　"Oh. but --" Carolina began.
　　"Go for it, Roberto," said Diego stubbornly.
　　Roberto waited for the rest of the class to disappear, then headed nervously for Professor Romero's desk.
　　"Yes, Gonzales?" Roberto took a deep breath.
　　"Professor, my aunt and uncle -- er -- forgot to sign my form," he said.
　　Professor Romero looked over her square spectacles at him but didn't say anything.
　　"So -- er d'you think it would be all right mean, will It be okay if I -- if I go to Hogsmeade?"
　　Professor Romero looked down and began shuffling papers on her desk.
　　"I'm afraid not, Gonzales," she said. "You heard what I said. No form, no visiting the village. That's the rule."
　　"But -- Professor, my aunt and uncle -- you know, they're Muggles, they don't really understand about -- about Hogwarts forms and stuff," Roberto said, while Diego egged him on with vigorous nods. "If you said I could go --"
　　"But I don't say so," said Professor Romero, standing up and piling her papers neatly into a drawer. "The form clearly states that the parent or guardian must give permission." She turned to look at him, with an odd expression on her face. Was it pity? "I'm sorry, Gonzales, but that's my final word. You had better hurry, or you'll be late for your next lesson."
　　There was nothing to be done. Diego called Professor Romero a lot of names that greatly annoyed Carolina; Carolina assumed an "all-for-the-best" expression that made Diego even angrier, and Roberto had to endure everyone in the class talking loudly and happily about what they were going to do first, once they got into Hogsmeade.
　　"There's always the feast," said Diego, in an effort to cheer Roberto UP. "You know, the Halloween feast, in the evening."
　　"Yeah," said Roberto gloomily, "great."
　　The Halloween feast was always good, but it would taste a lot better if he was coming to it after a day in Hogsmeade with everyone else. Nothing anyone said made him feel any better about being left behind. Dean Thomas, who was good with a quill, had offered to forge Uncle Francisco's signature on the form, but as Roberto had already told Professor Romero he hadn't had it signed, that was no good. Diego halfheartedly suggested the Invisibility Cloak, but Carolina stamped on that one, reminding Diego what Dominguez had told them about the dementors being able to see through them. Miguel had what were possibly the least helpful words of comfort.
　　"They make a fuss about Hogsmeade, but I assure you, Roberto, it's not all it's cracked up to be," he said seriously. "All right, the sweetshop's rather good, and Zonko's Joke Shop's frankly dangerous, and yes, the Shrieking Shack's always worth a visit, but really, Roberto, apart from that, you're not missing anything."
　　On Halloween morning, Roberto awoke with the rest and went down to breakfast, feeling thoroughly depressed, though doing his best to act normally.
　　"We'll bring you. lots of sweets back from Honeydukes," said Carolina, looking desperately sorry for him.
　　"Yeah, loads," said Diego. He and Carolina had finally forgotten their squabble about Crookshanks in the face of Roberto's difficulties.
　　"Don't worry about me," said Roberto, in what he hoped was at, offhand voice, "I'll see you at the feast. Have a good time."
　　He accompanied them to the entrance hall, where Filch, the caretaker, was standing inside the front doors, checking off names against a long list, peering suspiciously into every face, and making sure that no one was sneaking out who shouldn't be going.
　　"Staying here, Gonzales?" shouted Sosa, who was standing in line with Crabbe and Goyle. "Scared of passing the dementors?"
　　Roberto ignored him and made his solitary way up the marble staircase, through the deserted corridors, and back to Gryffindor Tower.
　　"Password?" said the Fat Lady, jerking out of a doze.
　　"Fortuna Major," said Roberto listlessly.
　　The portrait swung open and he climbed through the hole into the common room. It was full of chattering first and second years, and a few older students, who had obviously visited Hogsmeade so often the novelty had worn off
　　"Roberto! Roberto! Hi, Roberto!"
　　It was Colin Creevey, a second year who was deeply in awe of Roberto and never missed an opportunity to speak to him.
　　"Aren't you going to Hogsmeade, Roberto? Why not? Hey" -- Colin looked eagerly around at his friends -- "you can come and sit with us, if you like, Roberto!"
　　"Er -- no, thanks, Colin," said Roberto, who wasn't in the mood to have a lot of people staring avidly at the scar on his forehead. "I -- I've got to go to the library, got to get some work done."
　　After that, he had no choice but to turn right around and head back out of the portrait hole again.
　　"What was the point waking me up?" the Fat Lady called grumpily after him as he walked away.
　　Roberto wandered dispiritedly toward the library, but halfway there he changed his mind; he didn't feel like working. He turned around and came face-to-face with Filch, who had obviously just seen off the last of the Hogsmeade visitors.
　　"What are you doing?" Filch snarled suspiciously.
　　"Nothing," said Roberto truthfully.
　　"Nothing!" spat Filch, his jowls quivering unpleasantly. "A likely story! Sneaking around on your own -- why aren't you in Hogsmeade buying Stink Pellets and Belch Powder and Whizzing Worms like the rest of your nasty little friends?"
　　Roberto shrugged.
　　"Well, get back to your common room where you belong!" snapped Filch, and he stood glaring until Roberto had passed out of sight.
　　But Roberto didn't go back to the common room; he climbed a staircase, thinking vaguely of visiting the Owlery to see Hedwig, and was walking along another corridor when a voice from inside one of the rooms said, "Roberto?"
　　Roberto doubled back to see who had spoken and met Professor Cardozo, looking around his office door.
　　"What are you doing?" said Cardozo, though in a very different voice from Filch. "Where are Diego and Carolina?"
　　"Hogsmeade," said Roberto, in a would-be casual voice.
　　"Ah," said Cardozo. He considered Roberto for a moment. "Why don't you come in? I've just taken delivery of a grindylow for our next lesson." "A what?" said Roberto. I
　　He followed Cardozo into his office. In the corner stood a very large tank of water. A sickly green creature with sharp little horns had its face pressed against the glass, pulling faces and flexing its long, spindly fingers.
　　"Water demon," said Cardozo, surveying the grindylow thoughtfully. "We shouldn't have much difficulty with him, not after the kappas. The trick is to break his grip. You notice the abnormally long fingers? Strong, but very brittle."
　　The grindylow bared its green teeth and then buried itself in a tangle of weeds in a corner.
　　"Cup of tea?" Cardozo said, looking around for his kettle. "I was just thinking of making one."
　　"All right," said Roberto awkwardly.
　　Cardozo tapped the kettle with his wand and a blast of steam issued suddenly from the spout.
　　"Sit down," said Cardozo, taking the lid off a dusty tin. "I've only got teabags, I'm afraid -- but I daresay you've had enough of tea leaves?"
　　Roberto looked at him. Cardozo's eyes were twinkling.
　　"How did you know about that?" Roberto asked.
　　"Professor Romero told me," said Cardozo, passing Roberto a chipped mug of tea. "You're not worried, are you?"
　　"No," said Roberto.
　　He thought for a moment of telling Cardozo about the dog he'd seen in Magnolia Crescent but decided not to. He didn't want Cardozo to think he was a coward, especially since Cardozo alreadv seemed to think he couldn't cope with a boggart.
　　Something of Roberto's thoughts seemed to have shown on his face, because Cardozo said, "Anything worrying you, Roberto?"
　　"No," Roberto lied. He drank a bit of tea and watched the grindylow brandishing a fist at him. "Yes," he said suddenly, putting his tea down on Cardozo's desk. "You know that day we fought the boggart?"
　　"Yes," said Cardozo slowly.
　　"Why didn't you let me fight it?" said Roberto abruptly.
　　Cardozo raised his eyebrows.
　　"I would have thought that was obvious, Roberto," he said, sounding surprised.
　　Roberto, who had expected Cardozo to deny that he'd done any such thing, was taken aback.
　　"Why?" he said again.
　　"Well," said Cardozo, frowning slightly, "I assumed that if the boggart faced you, it would assume the shape of Lord Salvador."
　　Roberto stared. Not only was this the last answer he'd expected, but Cardozo had said Salvador's name. The only person Roberto had ever heard say the name aloud (apart from himself) was Professor Dominguez.
　　"Clearly, I was wrong," said Cardozo, still frowning at Roberto. "But I didn't think it a good idea for Lord Salvador to materialize in the staffroom. I imagined that people would panic."
　　"I didn't think of Salvador," said Roberto honestly. "I -- I remembered those dementors."
　　"I see," said Cardozo thoughtfully. "Well, well... I'm impressed." fie smiled slightly at the look of surprise on Roberto's face. "That suggests that what you fear most of all is -- fear. Very wise, Roberto."
　　Roberto didn't know what to say to that, so he drank some mot,, tea.
　　"So you've been thinking that I didn't believe you capable of fighting the boggart?" said Cardozo shrewdly.
　　"Well... yeah," said Roberto. He was suddenly feeling a lot happier. "Professor Cardozo, you know the dementors --"
　　He was interrupted by a knock on the door.
　　"Come in," called Cardozo.
　　The door opened, and in came Peralta. He was carrying a goblet, which was smoking faintly, and stopped at the sight of Roberto, his black eyes narrowing.
　　"Ah, Carlos," said Cardozo, smiling. "Thanks very much. Could you leave it here on the desk for me?"
　　Peralta set down the smoking goblet, his eyes wandering between Roberto and Cardozo.
　　"I was just showing Roberto my grindylow," said Cardozo pleasantly, pointing at the tank.
　　"Fascinating," said Peralta, without looking at it. "You should drink that directly, Cardozo."
　　"Yes, Yes, I will," said Cardozo.
　　"I made an entire cauldronful," Peralta continued. "If you need more.
　　"I should probably take some again tomorrow. Thanks very much, Carlos."
　　"Not at all," said Peralta, but there was a look in his eye Roberto didn't like. He backed out of the room, unsmiling and watchful.
　　Roberto looked curiously at the goblet. Cardozo smiled.
　　"Professor Peralta has very kindly concocted a potion for me," he said. "I have never been much of a potion-brewer and this one is particularly complex." He picked up the goblet and sniffed it. "Pity sugar makes it useless," he added, taking a sip and shuddering.
　　"Why --?" Roberto began. Cardozo looked at him and answered the unfinished question.
　　"I've been feeling a bit off-color," he said. "This potion is the only thing that helps. I am very lucky to be working alongside Professor Peralta; there aren't many wizards who are up to making it."
　　Professor Cardozo took another sip and Roberto had a crazy urge to knock the goblet out of his hands.
　　"Professor Peralta's very interested in the Dark Arts, he blurted out.
　　"Really?" said Cardozo, looking only mildly interested as he took another gulp of potion.
　　"Some people reckon --" Roberto hesitated, then plunged recklessly on, "some people reckon he'd do anything to get the Defense Against the Dark Arts job."
　　Cardozo drained the goblet and pulled a face.
　　"Disgusting," he said. "Well, Roberto, I'd better get back to work. see you at the feast later."
　　"Right," said Roberto, putting down his empty teacup.
　　The empty goblet was still smoking.
　　"There you go," said Diego. "We got as much as we could carry."
　　A shower of brilliantly colored sweets fell into Roberto's lap. It was dusk, and Diego and Carolina had just turned up in the common room, pink-faced from the cold wind and looking as though they'd had the time of their lives.
　　"Thanks," said Roberto, picking up a packet of tiny black Pepper Imps. "What's Hogsmeade like? Where did you go?"
　　By the sound of it -- everywhere. Dervish and Banges, the wizarding equipment shop, Zonko's Joke Shop, into the Three Broomsticks for foaming mugs of hot butterbeer, and many places besides.
　　"The post office, Roberto! About two hundred owls, all sitting on shelves, all color-coded depending on how fast you want your letter to get there!"
　　"Honeydukes has got a new kind of fudge; they were giving out free samples, there's a bit, look --"
　　"We think we saw an ogre, honestly, they get all sorts at the Three Broomsticks --"
　　"Wish we could have brought you some butterbeer, really warms you up --"
　　"What did you do?" said Carolina, looking anxious. "Did you get any work done?"
　　"No," said Roberto. "Cardozo made me a cup of tea in his office. And then Peralta came in...."
　　He told them all about the goblet. Diego's mouth fell open.
　　"Cardozo drank it?" he gasped. "Is he mad?"
　　Carolina checked her watch.
　　"We'd better go down, you know, the feast'll be starting in fiveminutes They hurried through the portrait hole and into the crowd, still discussing Peralta.
　　"But if he -- you know" -- Carolina dropped her voice, glancing nervously around -- "if he was trying to to poison Cardozo -- he wouldn't have done it in front of Roberto."
　　"Yeah, maybe," said Roberto as they reached the entrance hall and crossed into the Great Hall. It had been decorated with hundreds and hundreds of candle-filled pumpkins, a cloud of fluttering live bats, and many flaming orange streamers, which were swimming lazily across the stormy ceiling like brilliant watersnakes.
　　The food was delicious; even Carolina and Diego, who were full to bursting with Honeydukes sweets, managed second helpings of everything. Roberto kept glancing at the staff table. Professor Cardozo
　　looked cheerful and as well as he ever did; he was talking animatedly to tiny little Professor Flitwick, the Charms teacher. Roberto moved his eyes along the table, to the place where Peralta sat. Was he imagining it, or were Peralta's eyes flickering toward Cardozo more often than was natural?
　　The feast finished with an entertainment provided by the Hogwarts ghosts. They popped out of the walls and tables to do a bit of formation gliding; Nearly Headless Nick, the Gryffindor ghost, had a great success with a reenactment of his own botched beheading.
　　It had been such a pleasant evening that Roberto's good mood couldn't even be spoiled by Sosa, who shouted through the crowd as they all left the hall, "The dementors send their love, Gonzales!"
　　Roberto, Diego, and Carolina followed the rest of the Gryffindors along the usual path to Gryffindor Tower, but when they reached the corridor that ended with the portrait of the Fat Lady, they found it jammed with students.
　　"Why isn't anyone going in?" said Diego curiously.
　　Roberto peered over the heads in front of him. The portrait seemed to be closed.
　　"Let me through, please," came Miguel's voice, and he came bustling importantly through the crowd. "What's the holdup here? You can't all have forgotten the password -- excuse me, I'm Head Boy --"
　　And then a silence fell over the crowd, from the front first, so that a chill seemed to spread down the corridor. They heard Miguel say, in a suddenly sharp voice, "Somebody get Professor Dominguez. Quick."
　　People's heads turned; those at the back were standing on tiptoe.
　　"What's going on?" said Martina, who had just arrived.
　　A moment later, Professor Dominguez was there, sweeping toward the portrait; the Gryffindors squeezed together to let him through, and Roberto, Diego, and Carolina moved closer to see what the trouble was.
　　"Oh, my --" Carolina grabbed Roberto's arm.
　　The Fat Lady had vanished from her portrait, which had been slashed so viciously that strips of canvas littered the floor; great chunks of it had been torn away completely.
　　Dominguez took one quick look at the ruined painting and turned, his eyes somber, to see Professors Romero, Cardozo, and Peralta hurrying toward him.
　　"We need to find her," said Dominguez. "Professor Romero, please go to Mr. Filch at once and tell him to search every painting in the castle for the Fat Lady."
　　"You'll be lucky!" said a cackling voice.
　　It was Peeves the Poltergeist, bobbing over the crowd and looking delighted, as he always did, at the sight of wreckage or worry.
　　"What do you mean, Peeves?" said Dominguez calmly, and Peeves's grin faded a little. He didn't dare taunt Dominguez. Instead he adopted an oily voice that was no better than his cackle. "Ashamed, Your Headship, sit. Doesn't want to be seen. She's a horrible mess. Saw her running through the landscape up on the fourth floor, sir, dodging between the trees. Crying something dreadful," he said happily. "Poor thing," he added unconvincingly.
　　"Did she say who did it?" said Dominguez quietly.
　　"Oh yes, Professorhead," said Peeves, with the air of one cradling a large bombshell in his arms. "He got very angry when she wouldn't let him in, you see." Peeves flipped over and grinned at Dominguez from between his own legs. "Nasty temper he's got, that Gustavo Martinez."
　　CHAPTER NINE
　　GRIM DEFEAT
　　Professor Dominguez sent all the Gryffindors back to the Great Hall, where they were joined ten minutes later by the students from Hufflepuff, Ravenclaw, and Slytherin, who all looked extremely confused.
　　"The teachers and I need to conduct a thorough search of the castle," Professor Dominguez told them as Professors Romero and Flitwick closed all doors into the hall. "I'm afraid that, for your own safety, you will have to spend the night here. I want the prefects to stand guard over the entrances to the hall and I am leaving the Head Boy and Girl in charge. Any disturbance should be reported to me immediately," he added to Miguel, who was looking immensely proud and important. "Send word with one of the ghosts."
　　Professor Dominguez paused, about to leave the hall, and said, "Oh, yes, you'll be needing..."
　　One casual wave of his wand and the long tables flew to the edges of the hall and stood themselves against the walls; another wave, and the floor was covered with hundreds of squashy purple sleeping bags.
　　"Sleep well," said Professor Dominguez, closing the door behind him.
　　The hall immediately began to buzz excitedly; the Gryffindors were telling the rest of the school what had just happened.
　　"Everyone into their sleeping bags!" shouted Miguel. "Come on, now, no more talking! Lights out in ten minutes!"
　　"C'mon," Diego said to Roberto and Carolina; they seized three sleeping bags and dragged them into a corner.
　　"Do you think Martinez's still in the castle?" Carolina whispered anxiously.
　　"Dominguez obviously thinks he might be," said Diego.
　　"It's very lucky he picked tonight, you know," said Carolina as they climbed fully dressed into their sleeping bags and propped themselves on their elbows to talk. "The one night we weren't in the tower...."
　　I reckon he's lost track of time, being on the run," said Diego. "Didn't realize it was Halloween. Otherwise he'd have come bursting in here."
　　Carolina shuddered.
　　All around them, people were asking one another the same question: "How did he get in?"
　　"Maybe he knows how to Apparate," said a Ravenclaw a few feet away, "Just appear out of thin air, you know."
　　"Disguised himself, probably," said a Hufflepuff fifth year. "He could've flown in," suggested Dean Thomas.
　　"Honestly, am I the only person who's ever bothered to read Hogwarts, A History?" said Carolina crossly to Roberto and Diego.
　　"Probably," said Diego. "Why?"
　　"Because the castle's protected by more than walls, You know,,, said Carolina. "There are all sorts of enchantments on it, to stop people entering by stealth. You can't just Apparate in here. And I'd like to see the disguise that could fool those dementors. They're guarding every single entrance to the grounds. They'd have seen him fly in too. And Fitch knows all the secret passages, they'll have them covered...."
　　"The lights are going out now!" Miguel shouted. "I want everyone in their sleeping bags and no more talking!"
　　The candles all went out at once. The only light now came from the silvery ghosts, who were drifting about talking seriously to the prefects, and the enchanted ceiling, which, like the sky outside, was scattered with stars. What with that, and the whispering that still filled the hall, Roberto felt as though he were sleeping outdoors in a light wind.
　　Once every hour, a teacher would reappear in the hall to check that everything was quiet. Around three in the morning, when many students had finally fallen asleep, Professor Dominguez came in. Roberto watched him looking around for Miguel, who had been prowling between the sleeping bags, telling people off for talking. Miguel was only a short way away from Roberto, Diego, and Hermlone, who quickly pretended to be asleep as Dominguez's footsteps drew nearer.
　　"Any sign of him, Professor?" asked Miguel in a whisper.
　　"No. All well here?"
　　"Everything under control, sir."
　　"Good. There's no point moving them all now. I've found a temporary guardian for the Gryffindor portrait hole. You'll be able to move them back in tomorrow."
　　"And the Fat Lady, sir?"
　　"Hiding in a map of Argyllshire on the second floor. Apparently she refused to let Martinez in without the password, so he attacked. She's still very distressed, but once she's calmed down, I'll have Mr. Filch restore her."
　　Roberto heard the door of the hall creak open again, and more footsteps.
　　"Headmaster?" It was Peralta. Roberto kept quite still, listening hard. "The whole of the third floor has been searched. He's not there. And Filch has done the dungeons; nothing there either."
　　"What about the Astronomy tower? Professor Trelawney's room? The Owlery?"
　　"All searched."
　　"Very well, Carlos. I didn't really expect Martinez to linger."
　　"Have you any theory as to how he got in, Professor?" asked Peralta.
　　Roberto raised his head very slightly off his arms to free his other ear,
　　"Many, Carlos, each of them as unlikely as the next."
　　Roberto opened his eyes a fraction and squinted up to where they stood; Dominguez's back was to him, but he could see Miguel's face, rapt with attention, and Peralta's profile, which looked angry.
　　"You remember the conversation we had, Headmaster, just before -- ah -- the start of term?" said Peralta, who was barely opening his lips, as though trying to block Miguel out of the conversation.
　　"I do, Carlos," said Dominguez, and there was something like warning in his voice.
　　"It seems -- almost impossible -- that Martinez could have entered the school without inside help. I did express my concerns whet, you appointed --"
　　"I do not believe a single person inside this castle would have helped Martinez enter it," said Dominguez, and his tone made it so clear that the subject was closed that Peralta didn't reply. "I must go down to the dementors," said Dominguez. I said I would inform them when our search was complete."
　　"Didn't they want to help, sit?" said Miguel.
　　"Oh yes," said Dominguez coldly. "But I'm afraid no dementor will cross the threshold of this castle while I am headmaster."
　　Miguel looked slightly abashed. Dominguez left the hall, walking quickly and quietly. Peralta stood for a moment, watching the headmaster with an expression of deep resentment on his face; then he too left.
　　Roberto glanced sideways at Diego and Carolina. Both of them had their eyes open too, reflecting the starry ceiling.
　　"\What was all that about?" Diego mouthed.
　　The school talked of nothing but Gustavo Martinez for the next few days. The theories about how he had entered the castle became wilder and wilder; Hannah Abbott, from Hufflepuff, spent much of their next Herbology class telling anyone who'd listen that Martinez could turn into a flowering shrub.
　　The Fat Lady's ripped canvas had been taken off the wall and
　　Replaced with the portrait of Sir Cadogan and his fat gray pony. Nobody was very happy about this. Sir Cadogan spent half his time challenging people to duels, and the rest thinking up ridiculously complicated passwords, which he changed at least twice a day.
　　"He's a complete lunatic," said Seamus Finnigan angrily to Miguel. "Can't we get anyone else?"
　　"None of the other pictures wanted the job," said Miguel. "Frightened of what happened to the Fat Lady. Sir Cadogan was the only one brave enough to volunteer."
　　Sir Cadogan, however, was the least of Roberto's worries. He was now being closely watched. Teachers found excuses to walk along corridors with him, and Miguel Lopez (acting, Roberto suspected, on his mother's orders) was tailing him everywhere like an extremely pompous guard dog. To cap it all, Professor Romero summoned Roberto into her office, with such a somber expression on her face Roberto thought someone must have died.
　　"There's no point hiding it from you any longer, Gonzales," she said in a very serious voice. "I know this will come as a shock to you, but Gustavo Martinez --"
　　"I know he's after me," said Roberto wearily. "I heard Diego's dad telling his mum. Mr. Lopez works for the Ministry of Magic."
　　Professor Romero seemed very taken aback. She stared at Roberto for a moment or two, then said, "I see! Well, in that case, Gonzales, you'll understand why I don't think it's a good idea for you to be practicing Quidditch in the evenings. Out on the field with only Your team members, it's very exposed, Gonzales --"
　　"We've got our first match on Saturday!" said Roberto, outraged. "I've got to train, Professor!"
　　Professor Romero considered him intently. Roberto knew she was deeply interested in the Gryffindor team's prospects; it had been she, after all, who'd suggested him as Seeker in the first Place. He waited, holding his breath.
　　"Hmm..." Professor Romero stood up and stared out of the window at the Quidditch field, just visible through the rain. "Well... goodness knows, I'd like to see us win the Cup at last... but all the same, Gonzales... I'd be happier if a teacher were present. I'll ask Madam Hooch to oversee your training sessions."
　　The weather worsened steadily as the first Quidditch match drew nearer. Undaunted, the Gryffindor team was training harder than ever under the eye of Madam Hooch. Then, at their final training session before Saturday's match, Oliver Wood gave his team some unwelcome news.
　　"We're not playing Slytherin!" he told them, looking very angry. "Flint's just been to see me. We're playing Hufflepuff instead."
　　"Why?" chorused the rest of the team.
　　"Flint's excuse is that their Seeker's arm's still injured," said Wood, grinding his teeth furiously. "But it's obvious why they're doing it. Don't want to play in this weather. Think it'll damage their chances...."
　　There had been strong winds and heavy rain all day, and as Wood spoke, they heard a distant rumble of thunder.
　　"There's nothing wrong with Sosa's arm!" said Roberto furiously. "He's faking it!"
　　"I know that, but we can't prove it," said Wood bitterly, "And we've been practicing all those moves assuming we're playing Slytherin, and instead it's Hufflepuff, and their style's quite different. They've got a new Captain and Seeker, Nicolas Ortega --"
　　Angelina, Alicia, and Katie suddenly giggled.
　　"What?" said Wood, frowning at this lighthearted behavior.
　　"He's that tall, good-looking one, isn't he?" said Angelina.
　　"Strong and silent," said Katie, and they started to giggle again.
　　"He's only silent because he's too thick to string two words together," said Pablo impatiently. "I don't know why you're worried, Oliver, Hufflepuff is a pushover. Last time we played them, Roberto caught the Snitch in about five minutes, remember?"
　　"We were playing in completely different conditions!" Wood shouted, his eyes bulging slightly. "Ortega's put a very strong side together! He's an excellent Seeker! I was afraid you'd take it like this! We mustn't relax! We must keep our focus! Slytherin is trying to wrong-foot us! We must win!"
　　"Oliver, calm down!" said Pablo, looking slightly alarmed. "We're taking Hufflepuff very seriously. Seriously."
　　The day before the match, the winds reached howling point and the rain fell harder than ever. It was so dark inside the corridors and classrooms that extra torches and lanterns were lit. The Slytherin team was looking very smug indeed, and none more so than Sosa.
　　"Ah, if only my arm was feeling a bit better!" he sighed as the gale outside pounded the windows.
　　Roberto had no room in his head to worry about anything except the match tomorrow. Oliver Wood kept hurrying up to him between classes and giving him tips. The third time this happened, Wood talked for so long that Roberto suddenly realized he was ten minutes late for Defense Against the Dark Arts, and set off at a run with Wood shouting after him, "Ortega's got a very fast swerve, Roberto, so you might want to try looping him --"
　　Roberto skidded to a halt outside the Defense Against the Dark Arts classroom, pulled the door open, and dashed inside.
　　"Sorry I'm late, Professor Cardozo. I --"
　　But it wasn't Professor Cardozo who looked up at him from the teacher's desk; it was Peralta.
　　"This lesson began ten minutes ago, Gonzales, so I think we'll make it ten points from Gryffindor. Sit down."
　　But Roberto didn't move.
　　"Where's Professor Cardozo?" he said.
　　"He says he is feeling too ill to teach today," said Peralta with a twisted smile. "I believe I told you to sit down?"
　　But Roberto stayed where he was.
　　"What's wrong with him?"
　　Peralta's black eyes glittered.
　　"Nothing life-threatening," he said, looking as though he wished it were. "Five more points from Gryffindor, and if I have to ask you to sit down again, it will be fifty."
　　Roberto walked slowly to his seat and sat down. Peralta looked around at the class.
　　"As I was saying before Gonzales interrupted, Professor Cardozo has not left any record of the topics you have covered so far --"
　　"Please, sir, we've done boggarts, Red Caps, kappas, and grindylows," said Carolina quickly, "and we're just about to start --"
　　"Be quiet," said Peralta coldly. "I did not ask for information. I was merely commenting on Professor Cardozo's lack of organization."
　　"He's the best Defense Against the Dark Arts teacher we've ever had," said Dean Thomas boldly, and there was a murmur of agreement from the rest of the class. Peralta looked more menacing than ever.
　　"You are easily satisfied. Cardozo is hardly overtaxing you -- I ,Would expect first years to be able to deal with Red Caps and grindylows. Today we shall discuss --"
　　Roberto watched him flick through the textbook, to the very back chapter, which he must know they hadn't covered.
　　"Werewolves," said Peralta.
　　"But, sir," said Carolina, seemingly unable to restrain herself, "we're not supposed to do werewolves yet, we're due to start hinkypunks --"
　　"Miss Fernandez," said Peralta in a voice of deadly calm, "I was under the impression that I am teaching this lesson, not you. And I am telling you all to turn to page 394." He glanced around again. 'All of you! Now!"
　　With many bitter sidelong looks and some sullen muttering, the class opened their books.
　　"Which of you can tell me how we distinguish between the werewolf and the true wolf?" said Peralta.
　　Everyone sat in motionless silence; everyone except Carolina, whose hand, as it so often did, had shot straight into the air.
　　"Anyone?" Peralta said, ignoring Carolina. His twisted smile was back. "Are you telling me that Professor Cardozo hasn't even taught you the basic distinction between --"
　　"We told you," said Parvati suddenly, "we haven't got as far as werewolves yet, we're still on --"
　　"Silence!" snarled Peralta. "Well, well, well, I never thought I'd meet a third-year class who wouldn't even recognize a werewolf when they saw one. I shall make a point of informing Professor Dominguez how very behind you all are...."
　　"Please, sir," said Carolina, whose hand was still in the air, "the werewolf differs from the true wolf in several small ways. The snout of the werewolf --"
　　"That is the second time you have spoken out of turn, Miss Fernandez," said Peralta coolly. "Five more points from Gryffindor for being an insufferable know-it-all."
　　Carolina went very red, put down her hand, and stared at the floor with her eyes full of tears. It was a mark of how much the class loathed Peralta that they were all glaring at him, because every one of them had called Carolina a know-it-all at least once, and Diego, who told Carolina she was a know-it-all at least twice a week, said loudly, "You asked us a question and she knows the answer! Why ask if you don't want to be told?"
　　The class knew instantly he'd gone too far. Peralta advanced on Diego slowly, and the room held its breath.
　　"Detention, Lopez," Peralta said silkily, his face very close to Diego's. "And if I ever hear you criticize the way I teach a class again, you will be very sorry indeed."
　　No one made a sound throughout the rest of the lesson. They sat and made notes on werewolves from the textbook, while Peralta prowled up and down the rows of desks, examining the work they had been doing with Professor Cardozo.
